Stock markets

12.4	

Technical and fundamental analysis

In order to carry out their investment decisions, investors are often resorting to various kinds of analysis. Two basic approaches have received particular attention over the last decades. As the name suggests, the advocates of the so-called â€œfundamental analysisâ€ start their analysis on the basis of an investigation of the balance sheet, the income statement and the cash flow statement of a company. In doing so, they try to form a view about the companyâ€™s â€œtrueâ€ (or â€œintrinsicâ€ or â€œfundamentalâ€) value. The reasoning is obvious. Should the companyâ€™s stock price trade below the intrinsic value, one might consider an investment (and under specific circumstances, vice versa). Some of the variables that fundamental analysts base their decisions upon are:163 â€¢	 the earnings per share, which represent the net profits of a company (i.e. after corporation tax and interest have been paid) divided by the number of shares; â€¢	 the dividends per share, which stand for the profits that are distributed to the shareholders, divided by the number of shares; â€¢	 the dividend yield, which is the dividend per share divided by the price of the share; â€¢	 the price-to-book ratio, which represents the ratio of the price per share divided by the book value of the company per share; â€¢	 the price-earnings ratio, which stands for the current price of the share divided by the earnings per share; â€¢	 the current ratio (i.e. current assets divided by current liabilities); â€¢	 the liquid asset ratio (i.e. current assets minus inventories divided by current liabilities) and many others more. By contrast, technical analysts believe that the relevant information on the companyâ€™s fundamentals is already embedded in the actual share price. The information that is then needed for trading is the correct timing and the latter can then be found mainly in the dynamics of the share price itself. Assuming that stock market history tends to repeat itself, the challenge lies in identifying past trends and patterns of prices and volumes that have been followed by particular price movements and can, therefore, be used for predictions. There are many technical analysis and trading techniques, including graphical and non-graphical ones. It is probably fair to say that price charts, such as for instance line charts, moving averages, candle stick charts and many more, are among the more frequently used techniques. It is also often claimed that the main difference between these two alternatives lies in the relevant time horizon. While fundamental analysis takes a more long-term view, technical analysis focuses rather on a time window of weeks, days or even shorter intervals. At the same time, it might well be the case, that a fundamentally-oriented investor makes use of technical analysis in order to derive the right timing for his investment. Seen from this perspective, the two approaches are not necessarily mutually exclusive.

12.5	

The Graham approach

Benjamin Graham (originally Grossbaum) was born in 1894 in London (United Kingdom) and died in 1976 in Aix-en-Provence (France). Graham is widely recognized as an important teacher in the area of investment analysis. Graham and his co-author David Dodd first formulated the fundamentals of their approach in a textbook entitled â€œSecurity Analysisâ€, which soon earned Graham the reputation of one of the â€œfathers of value investingâ€. Following Graham, in order to take an investment decision, the investor should form an explicit view about the â€œintrinsic valueâ€ of a firm, based on the careful study of a firmâ€™s financial statements and, thereby, especially on the firmâ€™s assets, earnings, dividends and its overall financial strength.164 After having determined this intrinsic value (which admittedly leaves considerable room for judgment), he suggested to invest into assets that would trade substantially below this intrinsic value. The latter difference is called the â€œmargin of safetyâ€ and represents a key concept in the world of value investing. In a later publication, Graham laid out the specific set of criteria to be followed by defensive investors in more detail.165 More explicitly, they referred to an adequate size (i.e. small companies with less than $100 million of annual sales should be excluded); strong financial conditions (i.e. current assets should be at least twice current liabilities and long-term debt should not exceed the net current assets); earnings stability (i.e. positive earnings for at least the last five years); a strong dividend record (i.e. uninterrupted dividend payments for at least the past 20 years); earnings growth (i.e. a minimum increase of at least one-third in earnings per share in the past 10 years); a moderate price-to-earnings ratio (i.e. the current price should not be more than 15 times average earnings for the past three years) and a moderate priceto-book-value ratio (i.e. the current price should not be more than 1Â½ times the last reported book value).
ïƒ¾ How to value a stock â€“ the Graham approach How should a stock precisely be valued? In his book â€œThe Intelligent Investorâ€, Graham suggested to derive the intrinsic value of a stock according to the following formula:166 (i)	

Value = [ EPS â‹… (8.5 + 2g) ] â‹… 4.4 / AAA

where g represents the expected annual earnings growth rate for the next ten years, EPS stands for the earnings per share for the last year, AAA is the current interest rate on AAA-rated corporate bonds, and (8.5+2g) denotes the so-called â€œearnings multiplierâ€.
Source: Lowe (1996, pp. 150ff).

Stock markets

The basic features of the investment process Ã  la Graham have been nicely illustrated using the metaphor of â€œMr. Marketâ€. Mr. Market (a figure who is meant to illustrate the typical behaviour of the stock market) appears every day knocking at the door of the investor offering to buy or sell shares at different prices. For most of the time, the prices quoted by Mr. Market seem to make sense, but from time to time, they deviate substantially from the value of the firm. The investor, however, is free to agree with the offer by Mr. Market and to trade with him or, alternatively, to ignore him completely. Mr. Market does not resent any rejection and will be back the following day to quote another price. The message to be taken away is that the investor should avoid being infected by the irrational behaviour of markets and rather seek to take advantage of it. Value investing seems to be a quite promising investment style as has been documented in an impressive way by a number of well-known US-investors, among them Walter Schloss, William J. Ruane, Irving Kahn, Martin J. Whitman, Seth Klarman and, of course, Charlie Munger and Warren Buffett.167

Broader trends in a specific stock market are often described in terms of a so-called â€œstock market indexâ€. Commonly followed indices in the euro area include for instance the Dow Jones EURO STOXX, which includes the 50 Blue Chips of the euro area, and the DAX, which comprises the 30 biggest Blue Chips in Germany.168

Indices may differ according to the number of stocks included and their weighting scheme. In priceweighted indices, such as, for instance, the Dow Jones Industrial Average, the prices of each stock determine the value of the index, which might, therefore, be subject to considerable variation in nervous markets. By contrast, market-value weighted or capitalisation-weighted indices, such as, for instance, the Hang Seng Index, take the size of the company into account. It is worth mentioning that some indices exist in parallel in different versions.

12.7	

Equity risk premium

Previous considerations have shown that not only the expected return but also the foreseeable risks of holding an asset form part of an investorâ€™s considerations and it can be expected that he would like to be compensated for a higher risk by higher returns. When comparing an investment into stocks to safer investments, the investor will generally consider the expected return as the sum of the risk-free rate and a risk premium to compensate him for the risk of a stock market investment, i.e. the so-called â€œequity risk premiumâ€. When the risk of investing into a particular stock (or a stock market index) increases, the size of the premium will also rise and, hence, the higher risk will be compensated for by a higher premium. Expressed in other words, the observed difference in returns between the investment in a specific stock (or a stock market index) and the investment into a risk-free government bond can be regarded as a measure of such an equity risk premium, which represents the additional compensation investors require for the inherent risk. Equity risk premia constitute key elements of most models in modern finance dealing with valuation issues.

12.8	

The concept of Beta

Suppose, you would make an attempt to investigate the relationship between the return of an individual stock and the return of a stock market index by means of the following regression: (12.7.1)	

Ri = Ä® + È• Â˜ RM + u

where R i stands for the return of an individual stock and R M denotes the return of market portfolio. It can then be shown that beta does not only measure the degree in which both returns differ, but also the degree in which volatilities differ.169

103
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Stock markets

More precisely, beta constitutes a statistical measure that expresses the volatility (or sensitivity) of a stockâ€™s price relative to the price movement of the overall market. The market itself must have a beta value of one since, by definition, it moves exactly equal to itself. In case an asset has a beta of zero, this would be equivalent to saying that its price is not at all correlated with the market (i.e. the asset is completely independent). A positive beta must be interpreted in a way that the asset generally tracks the market more or less, whereas a negative beta shows that the asset is inversely linked to market developments (i.e. the asset generally decreases in value if the market goes up). In reality, most stocks tend to have a beta value of less than, equal to, or greater than one. A stock with a beta of two generates returns that change, on average, by twice the magnitude of the overall marketâ€™s returns. This is equivalent to saying that when the market returns rises or falls by four percent, the stockâ€™s return will, on average, rise or fall by eight percent, respectively. It is easy to see that, while stocks with high betas have the potential for higher returns, they also tend to be more volatile and, therefore, riskier. By contrast, low betaÂ´stocks imply less risk, but also lower returns. Taken together, beta is an extremely useful tool to be considered when building a portfolio. For instance, if you want a more conservative portfolio of stocks to ride out an expected decline in the market, you might consider concentrating on stocks with low betas. If, by contrast, you are extremely bullish on the overall market, your focus might be more on high beta stocks

13	 Foreign exchange markets
13.1	 Learning objectives
In this chapter, we first try to understand the basic definitions of an exchange rate. We then proceed by exploring the concepts of the purchasing power parity and the interest rate parity and, more specifically, its two variants. Moreover, we make an attempt to distinguish between various exchange rate regimes and to evaluate the effects of exchange market interventions. Finally, we take a closer look at the key characteristica of the ERM II.

13.2	

Basic considerations

Basically all international transactions involve the use of exchange rates. In essence, an exchange rate denotes the price of one countryâ€™s currency expressed in another countryâ€™s currency. There are basically two methods of expressing such an exchange rate, namely first in domestic currency units per unit of foreign currency (e.g. in euro per US dollar or EUR/USD) and, second, in foreign currency units per unit of domestic currency (e.g. in US dollar per euro or USD/EUR). It is easy to see that the second method is merely the inverse of the former.

105
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Click on the ad to read more

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Foreign exchange markets

While it is not really of relevance, which method of expressing the exchange rate is applied, it matters when discussing about a rise or fall in the exchange rate, because the meaning will be very different depending on which definition is used. A rise in the euro per dollar exchange rate means that (from the perspective of a euro area resident) more euros have to be given to obtain one US dollar, that means that the euro has depreciated in value, or, equivalently, the dollar has appreciated in its value. If the second definition is employed, a rise in the exchange rate would mean that (from the perspective of a euro area resident) more US dollars are obtained per euro, so that the euro has appreciated or, equivalently, the US dollar has depreciated. In the following considerations, the second definition, i.e. the exchange rate as foreign currency per unit of domestic currency (e.g. in USD/EUR) â€“ will be used, in line with large parts of the literature. This definition offers the advantage that a rise in the exchange rate will coincide with an appreciation of the domestic currency (i.e. of the euro).170 In todayâ€™s financial markets, a variety of well-known symbols have been introduced which represent specific currencies. For instance, the symbol â€œ$â€ stands for (US) dollars, the symbol â€œâ‚¬â€represents (euro area) euros, the symbols â€œÂ£â€and â€œÂ¥â€ denote (British) pounds and (Japanese) yen, respectively. The table below lists some of the most popular international currency symbols and the (three letter) international standard (ISO 4217) codes widely used in the financial world.171

The use of these codes helps to eliminate any confusion regarding currency names and symbols that can occur during transactions. In this respect, an interesting development could be observed in early June 2012. All of a sudden, a new Bloomberg-code named â€œXGDâ€ (as compared to the old code standing for Greek Drachma before beginning of 2001) could be observed. The reason was that some Greek parties had announced to leave the euro area in case of victory and, hence, also to abandon the euro. Therefore, the markets fearing a Greek exit (a so-called â€œGrexitâ€) apparently prepared for the worst.172 Some other concepts are worth mentioning. The expression â€œspot exchange rateâ€ describes the current exchange rate, i.e. the rate for buying and selling currencies at this point in time. By contrast, the expression â€œforward exchange rateâ€ stands for the exchange rate that is quoted today, but for payment and delivery at a specific future date. The real exchange rate denotes the nominal exchange rate adjusted for relative prices between the countries under investigation.173 By contrast, the effective exchange rate illustrates whether a currency is appreciating or depreciating against a weighted basket of foreign currencies. Against the background of a situation, in which a currency is appreciating vis-Ã -vis some currencies and â€“ at the same time â€“ depreciating vis-Ã -vis some other currencies, an effective exchange rate in essence constitutes a useful tool to combine such divergent movements in (bilateral) exchange rates into an overall key (index) number.

13.3	

Purchasing power parity

In the previous chapter, we introduced various exchange rate concepts and discussed their advantages and disadvantages. In this chapter, we will proceed by having a closer look at the fundamentals driving exchange rates. More specifically, we will derive one of the earliest and simplest models of exchange rate determination, which is widely known as the so-called â€œpurchasing power parityâ€ (â€œPPPâ€) theory. Although it is widely acknowledged that the purchasing power parity was first developed by the Swedish economist Gustav Cassel (1866â€“1945) in 1918, it is often claimed that its origins date back to the writings of the British economist David Ricardo (1772â€“1823).174 In essence, the purchasing power parity is based on the idea of the so-called â€œlaw of one priceâ€. The latter states that in a market that can be characterised by competitive pressures and the absence of transport costs and tariffs or any kind of other barriers to trade, identical goods must have one price. In case of deviations, arbitrage forces will equalise goods prices once the price of goods is measured in the same currency. Such arbitrage occurs when economic agents exploit price differences to realise a riskless profit by buying the good in the marketplace, where it is cheaper, and selling it in the marketplace, where a higher price can be obtained.

107
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Foreign exchange markets

While the literature often refers to â€œtheâ€ purchasing power parity theory, it is fair to say that several variants do exist in parallel. The so-called â€œabsolute versionâ€ of the purchasing power parity claims that if the price of a bundle of goods in the home country is compared to the price of an identical bundle of goods sold in a foreign currency (converted by the exchange rate into a common currency of measurement), then prices will be equal. For example, if a bundle of goods costs â‚¬100 in the euro area and the same bundle costs $200 in the United States, then the exchange rate (defined as $ per â‚¬) will be equivalent to 200 / 100 = 2.00 $/â‚¬. In algebraic terms, the absolute version of the purchasing power parity can be stated as:175 (13.3.1)	

S=

PA P

where S denotes the exchange rate (defined in terms of foreign currency units per unit of domestic currency), P stands for the price of a bundle of goods in the home country (expressed in the domestic currency), and PA represents the price of an identical bundle of goods in the foreign country (expressed in terms of foreign currency).176 Following this version of the purchasing power parity, a rise in the home price level relative to the foreign price level will inevitably lead to a proportional decrease (and, thus, a proportional depreciation) of the home currency against the foreign currency. Taking up the aforementioned example, an increase in the price of the euro area bundle to â‚¬150 and an unchanged price of the US bundle, remaining at $200, will then result in a depreciation of the euro to 1.33 $ per â‚¬.

108
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Click on the ad to read more

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Foreign exchange markets

In light of the existence of transport costs, imperfect information and the distorting effect of tariffs and other forms of protectionism, it is often argued that it is not realistic to expect the absolute version of the purchasing power parity to hold. This notwithstanding, a weaker form of the purchasing power parity, widely known as the â€œrelative versionâ€ of the purchasing power parity, can be expected to hold even in the presence of such distortions. Expressed in simple terms, the relative version of purchasing power parity states that the exchange rate will adjust by the amount of the inflation differential between the two economies. In algebraic terms, this can be expressed as follows: (13.3.2)	

Ç» s = Ç» pA - Ç» p

where âˆ† s stands for the percentage change in the exchange rate (i.e. the change in logarithms, hence the small letters), âˆ† p represents the domestic inflation rate and âˆ† p A is the foreign inflation rate. According to this version of purchasing power parity, if the inflation rate in the euro area equals two percent while its equivalent in the US is four percent, the euro per dollar exchange rate should be expected to increase, that is to appreciate by approximately two percent. So far, we have also assumed the purchasing power parity to hold for all types of goods. A more subtle distinction for the purchasing power parity distinguishes between â€œtradableâ€ and â€œnon-tradableâ€ goods.177 Tradable goods are exposed to the headwinds of international competition, such as most manufactured goods, whereas non-tradable goods are those that cannot be traded internationally (for instance houses or certain services such as a haircut). The reason underlying such a distinction is that on a priori grounds, the purchasing power parity is more likely to hold for tradable than for non-tradable goods. The latter result is due to the fact that the prices of tradable goods will tend to be kept â€œunder controlâ€ by international competition, while the prices of non-tradable goods will be determined predominantly by domestic supply and demand considerations. What are the implications of such a distinction for purchasing power parity? Let us start by assuming that the theory of purchasing power parity only holds for tradable goods, which means that (13.3.3)	

PTR A = S â‹… PTR

where S represents the exchange rate, PTR stands for the price of tradable goods in the home country (measured in terms of the domestic currency) and PTR A is the price of tradable goods in the foreign country (in terms of the foreign currency).

109

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Foreign exchange markets

The aggregate price index ( P ) for the domestic currency consists of a weighted average of the prices of both tradable ( PTR ) and non-tradable goods ( PNT ), both being priced in domestic currency. Likewise, the foreign aggregate price index ( P A ) is made up of a weighted average of the prices of tradables ( PTR A ) and non-tradables ( PNT A ), both in terms priced in the foreign currency. This yields: (13.3.4)	

P = Ä® Â˜ PTR + (1 - Ä®) Â˜ PNT

where Î± is the proportion of tradable goods in the domestic price index. Moreover, for the aggregate price index in the foreign country, the following expression holds: (13.3.5)	

P A = È• Â˜ PTR A + (1 - È•) Â˜ PNT A

The last equation must be seen as an important modification to the simple purchasing power parity. Taken together, the overall evidence on the purchasing power parity theory is not very supportive, and the results show that, first, at least for some rates the deviations from purchasing power parity are both substantial and prolonged. Second, it can be shown that exchange rates have been much more volatile than the corresponding price levels.180 Third, from an empirical perspective, the purchasing power parity seems to hold better in the long run than in the short run. This is due to the fact that fundamentals and arbitrage must be seen as predominantly long-run economic forces. Fourth, purchasing power parity seems to hold better for tradable than for non-tradable goods.181

13.4	

Interest rate parity

The previous chapter has shown that a long-run relationship between exchange rates and price levels, the so-called â€œpurchasing power parityâ€, exists. The following paragraphs will focus on the analysis of the short- and medium-run relationship between exchange rates and interest rates and the arbitrage forces linking these variables. While earlier considerations have analysed the relationship of goods prices, this section focuses exclusively on financial investment opportunities. In todayâ€™s world, there is a variety of possible investments. For reasons of simplicity, we will â€“ at the current stage â€“ just focus on two alternative investments, namely a domestic asset and a foreign asset. We will further assume that the domestic and the foreign asset under consideration are â€œperfect substitutesâ€.182 Now suppose, for instance, that you have a certain amount (A) of euros to invest for one time period and you are only interested in the return in euros.183 What are your alternatives? Well, on the one hand, you can purchase a euro area bond. If the euro area interest rate is equal to iEA, your return at the end of the period (R) will be: (13.4.1)	

R = (1+ i EA ) â‹… A

An alternative investment would consist of buying a US bond. Since the latter is denominated in US dollars, such an investment would automatically ask for a number of transactions on the foreign exchange market. More precisely, the amount of A euros would first need to be converted into Aâ€†âˆ™â€†S US dollars on the spot market, where S represents the spot exchange rate of the US dollar at the current point in time. Second, the amount of Aâ€†âˆ™â€†S US dollars would need to be invested in US bonds. Now, if the US interest rate is equal to iUS , the return of the investment will be equal to (1 + iUS ) â‹… A â‹… S US dollars by the end of the period. You are, however, not interested in the return in US dollars, but only in the return in euros. In other words, you will have to convert these dollars at the end of the period back into euros. This poses a problem, because at the moment you are deciding about your investment, you do not know what the future spot exchange rate of the US dollar is going to be. Fortunately, the forward exchange market provides a solution. Making use of the forward rate (F), you can express your return in euros, which is: (13.4.2)	

R = (1+ i US ) â‹… (S / F) â‹… A

In sum, you exactly know the return to your investment, independent of the fact whether you choose to invest into euro area or US bonds and, like many other participants in financial markets, you will choose the asset with the highest return. As a matter of fact, if the two assets are perfect substitutes and both are held in equilibrium, the return of the two assets must be the same in order to ensure that the market does not prefer one asset over the other. Taken together, this yields the following equilibrium condition for the choice between an investment in domestic and in foreign bonds:184 (13.4.3)	 or, equivalently (13.4.4)	

(1+ i A ) â‹… (S / F) â‹… A = (1+ i) â‹… A

F 1+i A = S 1+ i

It goes without saying that the time horizon for all variables in the last equation must be consistent, so if the forward rate used is, for example, the three-month forward rate at the current juncture, then both the domestic and the foreign interest rates must also be represented by three-month interest rates.

This equation then states that the difference between the (logarithm of the) forward rate and the (logarithm of the) spot rate must be equal to the difference between the foreign and the domestic interest rate. In the literature, this expression is known as the so-called â€œcovered interest rate parity conditionâ€ because you have fully covered your exposure vis-Ã -vis the return in foreign currency on the forward exchange market. It provides a powerful and crucial relationship between interest rates on the one side and spot and forward exchange rates on the other side. It is worth mentioning, however, that one of these returns, namely the one on euro area bonds remains certain, whereas, the other return, namely the return on US bonds is subject to some uncertainty and, therefore, needs to be predicted. (1+ i EA ) â‹… A = (1 + i US ) â‹… (S / Se ) â‹… A

would hold as an equilibrium condition between an investment in domestic and in foreign bonds. In essence, this equation says that the difference in (logarithm of) the foreign and (the logarithm of) the domestic interest rate must be equal to the expected appreciation or depreciation of the foreign currency. As it stands, however, the equation cannot directly be used for empirical testing since it contains the expectation of the future exchange rate and the former cannot be measured directly.185 At the same time, in combination with the covered interest parity condition, however, it is rather easy to see that the forward exchange rate should be equal to the expected value of the future spot exchange rate: (13.4.9)

Starting with the empirics of the covered interest parity condition, an empirical investigation would ask for a number of issues to be taken properly into account. First, it has to be ensured that the home and foreign assets are indeed comparable in terms of maturity and default and political risk.186 Second, potential transaction costs need to be taken into consideration. Third, a number of possible econometric problems need to be avoided. How would such a test look like? A number of authors have tested the following relationship: (13.4.10)	
A f - s = Ä®1 + Ä® 2 Â˜ Âª Â¬i -i Âº Â¼+u

where f is the logarithm of the forward exchange rate, s is the logarithm of the spot exchange rate, i represents the domestic interest rate, i A denotes the foreign interest rate and u is the random error with zero mean and normal distribution. For the covered interest rate parity to hold, the restrictions of Î±1 = 0 and Î±2 = 1 would need to be tested.

Empirical tests seem to show that the covered interest parity almost perfectly holds.187 Against the background of the modern technical computer and communication equipment used today in modern foreign exchange dealing rooms, which â€“ as a consequence â€“ tend to make transaction costs small and covered interest parity indeed riskless, this result should not come as a real surprise. When comparing the returns from purchasing a euro area bond with the revenue from purchasing a US bond in the previous section, we noted that the euro area individual was exposed to foreign exchange risk when purchasing the US asset. In general, various types of risks, among them political and economic risks, do exist. At the same time, it seems quite plausible to assume that economic agents are â€œrisk averseâ€ that is, other things equal, they prefer less risk to more risk. The latter fact, however, does not imply that risky assets will not be held in equilibrium, but rather that risk-averse investors will demand a compensation for holding these assets. Such compensation is called a â€œrisk premiumâ€. In general, it can be expected that such a risk premium will rise if, first, the degree of risk aversion rises and, second, the perceived riskiness increases. Including both a risk premium and transaction costs into our previous considerations, the following relationship can be expected to hold: (13.4.11)	

i = i A - (s e - s) + risk premium + transaction costs

This equation indicates that the return from investing at home is equal to the return from investing abroad minus the expected appreciation (or depreciation) of the exchange rate plus a risk premium to compensate for the exposure to foreign exchange risk (if investing abroad) plus any transaction costs involved in foreign investments. Taken together, there is good reason to expect the uncovered interest rate parity condition not to hold perfectly. At the same time, there are, however, a number of reasons, why the uncovered interest rate parity may hold (at least approximately) for some markets.188 First, transaction costs can be shown to have declined considerably over time. In terms of the equation above, this would tend to narrow the band within which uncovered interest parity should hold. Second, for most of the regularly traded currencies we should expect the risk premium to be within reasonable (but non-zero) limits, which again narrows the band within which uncovered interest parity should hold. Third, and perhaps most importantly, the aforementioned equation was derived from a euro area perspective. For an economic subject located in the US, the euro area must be seen as the foreign country demanding a risk premium and involving transaction costs. Since actual observations are based on the aggregate behaviour of both euro area and US citizens, the deviation from uncovered interest parity caused by the aforementioned equation and its US counterpart should at least to some extent cancel out in the aggregation process, yet again narrowing the band, within which uncovered interest parity should hold. However, this does not mean that risk premia and transaction costs are not important for explaining aggregate behaviour.

s e - s = È•1 + È• 2 Â˜ >i EA -i US @ + u

It is easy to see that the test of the uncovered interest parity condition is clearly much more difficult than the test of its covered equivalent. This is mainly due to the fact that testing for the former condition involves the testing of the joint hypotheses of risk neutrality and a pre-specified form of expectations. In practice, it is often assumed that financial markets have rational expectations, which means that, on average, they forecast the correct value for the exchange rate. ln (s t+i ) = ln (s t+i e ) + u

Notwithstanding a number of specific empirical and econometric problems (such as, for instance the non-constancy and non-linearity of risk premia or the so-called â€œsimultaneity biasâ€), it is often found that the uncovered interest rate parity does not perfectly hold. Besides the expectations problem mentioned above, this result may be caused by the existence of transaction costs as well as by the fact that investors are not risk-neutral. A number of economists have recently argued in favour of another key feature of exchange rate markets that â€“ in their view â€“ has not been adequately reflected in the literature yet. In essence, the basic idea of the â€œcarry tradeâ€ phenomenon consists in exploiting existing interest rate differentials across countries and it seems a well-known fact that the strategy is widely used by investors in international financial markets. In its simplest version, an investor borrows a certain amount in a low-interest rate currency (the so-called â€œfundingâ€ currencyâ€), converts the funds into a high-interest rate (the so-called â€œtargetâ€ currency) and invests the resulting amount in the target currency at the high-interest rate.190 Some studies suspect that such behaviour can result in sizeable and quite persistent exchange rate movements.191 In particular, as this strategy involves the selling of the funding currency and the purchase of the target currency, this would lead to a depreciation of the former currency and an appreciation of the latter currency, which can â€“ due to its additional potential in raising profitability â€“ not only encourage further trades along those lines, but â€“ at the same time â€“ further amplify exchange rate movements. While the strategy apparently seems to be quite lucrative in â€œnormalâ€ times, in turbulent phases, it might prove necessary for investors to liquidate their holdings in the target currency and revert back to the funding currency, thus driving the target currency down and the funding currency up. If, however, the target currency then depreciates against the funding currency, the initial amount borrowed will basically increase (in terms of the target currency), thereby effectively increasing the borrowing costs and initiating further withdrawals out of the target currency. Against this background, foreign exchange markets might â€œgo insaneâ€ and apparently act against all economic intuition. Indeed, some studies seem to show that the behaviour of exchange rates, while often matching the â€œtraditionalâ€ theoryâ€ quite well, can over some periods be better explained by â€œcarry tradeâ€ considerations.192

13.5	

Exchange market interventions

The aforementioned equation can also be of help when assessing in more detail the effectiveness of exchange market interventions carried out by a central bank. According to our previous considerations, the following relationship can be expected to hold: (13.5.1)	 or alternatively, (13.5.2)	

i = i A - (s e - s) + risk premium + transaction costs

s = (i -i A ) + s e - risk premium

117
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Foreign exchange markets

when abstracting from transaction costs. A closer look then reveals that the overall effects of an exchange market intervention carried out by the central bank can be decomposed into three individual channels: (i) a risk premium effect, (ii) an interest rate differential effect and (iii) an expectations effect.193 The risk premium effect works as follows: a purchase of foreign currency against domestic currency by the central bank lowers (other things equal) the risk premium. The decline in risk makes the foreign currency more attractive, thus leading to an appreciation of the foreign and a depreciation of the domestic currency. Moreover, the interest rate differential effect works as follows: a purchase of foreign currency against domestic currency increases the domestic money supply and, thereby, lowers the domestic interest rate which, in turn, leads to a decline in the interest rate differential and, as a consequence, to a depreciation of the home currency. From this perspective, the interest rate differential effect reinforces the portfolio effect. Finally, the equation postulates a direct one-to-one relationship between the expected exchange rate and the actual exchange rate. This is equivalent to saying that a central bank that manages to change the expected exchange rate also influences the actual exchange rate. The latter effect can be said to be based on a kind of â€œinformation functionâ€ of the exchange rate intervention. Regarding this information function, this leads to an important distinction, namely the one between the â€œsystematicâ€ part of such information and the â€œinnovativeâ€ part of such information. It can be expected that sophisticated market participants have already included the systematic part in their information set. But the innovative part can, by definition, be regarded as â€œnewsâ€ and will, therefore, lead to an adjustment in the expected exchange rate. It is crucial, however, that the direction of this effect depends on the concrete interpretation of the â€œnewsâ€ by the market participants.

13.6	

Exchange rate regimes

The way an authority manages its own currency is reflected in the choice of the exchange rate regime. The two polar cases are a system of floating exchange rates and a system of fixed exchange rates. While in the latter case, the home currency is tied to another currency (or basket of currencies), in the former case the exchange rate determination is left to the foreign exchange market. Somewhere between these two solutions lies the so-called â€œmanaged floatâ€, in which the central bank keeps the value of the currency within a certain band. A variant of the latter approach is represented by the so-called â€œcrawling pegâ€, in which a predetermined rate of depreciation against the foreign currency is selected.

se âˆ’ s = i A âˆ’ i

Source: Blanchard (1997, p. 263).

In case of the so-called â€œdollarisationâ€, a sound foreign currency is used either in parallel to the home currency or all on its own. In the latter case, this avoids any kind of speculative attacks on the domestic currency (as it has ceased to exist).195 In case of a currency board, an explicit legislative commitment vis-Ã -vis a pre-specified foreign currency with a fixed exchange rate exists. This leads to a situation, in which the domestic currency is fully backed by a foreign currency and, therefore, can only expand when more foreign exchange reserves are available. This leaves relatively limited scope for discretionary policy to the home central bank.196

13.7	

ERM II

As already mentioned, at the current juncture nineteen countries have joined the euro area and, therefore, introduced the euro as their currency. The relationship between the euro and the currencies outside the euro area is laid down in the so-called â€œExchange Rate Mechanism (ERM II)â€. The ERM II was set up on 1 January 1999 and, thus, succeeded the ERM. Its main purpose is to help potential euro area members in their preparation for participation in the euro area and â€“ at the same time â€“ to create an evaluation mechanism for those countries. Participation in ERM II is voluntary although the convergence criterion on exchange rate stability clearly requires participation in the ERM II mechanism without severe tensions for at least two years.197 The main features of ERM II lie in the fact that the exchange rate of a non-euro area Member State is fixed against the euro and is only allowed to fluctuate within pre-set limits. More precisely, it involves: â€¢	 an agreement about a â€œcentralâ€ exchange rate between the euro and the countryâ€™s currency. The currency is then allowed to fluctuate by up to Â±15% above or below this central rate. â€¢	 when these limits are broken, the currency is supported by foreign exchange market interventions to keep the exchange rate against the euro within the Â±15% fluctuation band. Interventions are coordinated by the ECB and the central bank of the non-euro area Member State. â€¢	 countries participating within ERM II can unilaterally decide to maintain a narrower fluctuation band, but this decision has generally no impact on the official Â±15% fluctuation margin.198 In general, the co-ordination of the monetary and exchange-rate policies, and thus also the monitoring of ERM II and the related administration lies in the hands of the General Council, of course, in close cooperation with the member stateâ€™s central bank. At the current stage, the ERM II includes only the Danish kroner (with a narrow fluctuation band of Â±2.25%).

14	 Derivative markets
14.1	 Learning objectives
In this chapter, we first try to understand the key features of a typical derivative instrument. We then proceed by analyzing the characteristics of forward and futures contracts, swaps and options. Finally, we reflect upon the nature of credit default swaps.

14.2	

Basic considerations

This section focuses on derivative instruments (or â€œderivativesâ€), which are, broadly speaking, securities that derive their value from an underlying asset, such as, for instance, a stock or a bond. In essence, derivatives are intended to allow investors to benefit from price movements in the underlying asset. Derivatives generally include forward contracts, futures contracts, options and swaps. An important use of derivatives is â€œto hedgeâ€ against negative outcomes. Suppose, you are a farmer and worried about a possible decline in the price of wheat, when selling it in fall. At the same time, the miller you are doing business with is concerned about the price, he would have to pay in fall.199 The risks, the miller and yourself are facing could be reduced if you could agree on a price for the wheat delivery (in fall) already now and put it down into a contract. Both parties would benefit from such a transaction in the sense, that the risks they are facing are reduced. Against this background, hedging with futures can be seen as beneficial as it tends to reduce aggregate risk in the economy. However, derivatives can also be used â€œto speculateâ€, that is to bet on a specific outcome regarding asset price developments in the expectation of a substantial financial profit. Against this background, speculators are often regarded as being of less use for an economy, but as we will see in later paragraphs, this is not necessarily the case.

14.3	

Forward and futures contracts

Forward contracts are private agreements, where one party agrees to buy (or sell) one commodity at a specific price (i.e. the â€œforward priceâ€) on a specific future date and the other party agrees to sell (or buy).200 Under such an agreement, the underlying goods are actually delivered. In essence, nothing happens between the contracting date and the date of delivery as the payment only takes place on the date of delivery. However, unless both parties are financially strong, there is always a danger that one party will financially default in the contract (i.e. a â€œcounterparty riskâ€), especially if the price changes markedly after the agreement is reached.

122
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Derivative markets

A futures contract is similar to a forward contract but with some key differences. First, futures contracts are legally binding commitments. Second, futures contracts are generally standardized instruments that are traded on organised exchanges, whereas forward contracts are generally tailor-made, negotiated between the two parties, and not traded after they have been signed. Third, following the â€œmarking to marketâ€-methodology, the potential gains and losses are noted on daily basis and additional payments have to be made in case of losses. Fourth, in the case of futures, physical delivery of the underlying is virtually never taken â€“ the two parties simply settle up with cash for the difference between the contracted price and the actual price on the expiration date. As a matter of principle, the buyer is described as being â€œlongâ€, whereas the seller is called being â€œshortâ€. From a historical perspective, it is also worth noting that futures and forward contracts were originally developed for commodities such as, for instance, wheat. In todayâ€™s world, however, commodities contracts still play an important role, but far more trading takes place in foreign exchange and interest rate futures.

14.4	Swaps
As the name states, a swap represents an agreement between two parties (i.e. â€œcounterpartiesâ€) to swap something â€“ generally obligations to make specific payment flows. Nowadays, most swaps involve either interest payments or currencies. Swaps represent over-the-counter (OTC) contracts and, hence, expose the parties involved to counterparty risk.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Derivative markets

The payments involved in a swap are generally based on some predetermined principal amount (i.e. â€œnotional principal amountâ€). However, the only payments that are exchanged between the counterparties are the agreed-upon payments, not the notional amount. Suppose, for instance, the following constellation: the company A has 20-year, â‚¬200 million floating rate bond outstanding, while company B has â‚¬200 million, fixed rate issue outstanding over the same period. Therefore, each company has an obligation to carry out specific interest rate payments, but one payment is fixed, whereas the other one will vary in the future. Now for some reason, company B prefers to have a variable debt and vice versa. If the companies swapped their payment obligations, an interest rate swap would occur. Company A would now have to make fixed payments and company B would have a floating stream.201 Similarly, in the case of currency swaps, the counterparties swap the exchange rate risk.

14.5	Options
An option is a contract, in which the option seller grants the option buyer the right (but not the obligation) to enter into transaction on an underlying asset at some predetermined price within a specified period of time. The specified price is called the â€œstrike priceâ€ (or alternatively, the â€œexercise priceâ€) and the specified date is called the â€œexpiration dateâ€. The option seller is called the â€œoption writerâ€, whereas the option buyer is called the â€œoption holderâ€. The asset, that is the subject of the option, is called the â€œunderlyingâ€ and it can be an individual stock, a stock price index, a bond or any other derivative instrument. In case of a so-called â€œcall optionâ€, the option holder has the right to purchase the underlying. By contrast, in case of a â€œput optionâ€, the option holder has the right to sell the underlying. Moreover, a â€œEuropean optionâ€ can be exercised only at the expiration date of the contract, whereas an â€œAmerican optionâ€ can be exercised any time before the expiration date. A final terminological issue refers to the question, whether the strike price (or exercise price) is higher or lower than the actual stock price. When the strike price exceeds the current stock price, a call option is said to be â€œout-of-the-moneyâ€. If, by contrast, the exercise price is below the current stock price, the option is said to be â€œin the moneyâ€. Options are very important instruments in todayâ€™s financial markets and it is worth understanding their basics. Suppose you would buy a call option on the stock of company A for â‚¬10 and a strike price of â‚¬120. When the actual price of company A is between zero and â‚¬120, you would not exercise the option (i.e. you are â€œout-of-the-moneyâ€) and, thus, inevitably incur a loss of â‚¬10. When the price exceeds â‚¬120, you (being the option holder) are â€œin-the-moneyâ€ and will take advantage from exercising it. In case the price exceeds â‚¬130, you will earn a profit from exercising the option.

Derivative markets

In todayâ€™s financial world, options are not only available for individual stocks but also, for instance, for broader stock price indices. A further important difference refers to the question, whether you actually possess the stocks that you are selling. In case, these stocks are part of your portfolio, you are said to deal with â€œcovered optionsâ€, if this is not the case, your business refers to â€œnaked optionsâ€.

Derivative markets

14.6	

Credit default swaps

A credit default swap (CDS) is a financial swap arrangement in which the seller of the CDS commits to the buyer for compensation in the event of a loan default. It is for this reason that a CDS can be seen as similar to a credit insurance. This notwithstanding, the name CDS is a bit misleading since, in essence, the buyer then receives compensation (usually the face value of the loan) and the seller takes possession of the defaulted loan. Credit default swaps have been invented in the 1990s and their use has become widespread over the last decades. The corresponding price of such a CDS (i.e. the so-called â€œspreadâ€) is then equivalent to the annual amount that the protection buyer must pay to the protection seller over the length of the contract (generally expressed as a percentage of the notional amount). Payments are usually made on a quarterly basis and continue until either the CDS expires or the underlying entity defaults. For example, if the CDS-spread on company A is 50 basis points (or 0.5%), then an investor willing to insure an amount of â‚¬100 million, must pay the CDS-seller â‚¬500â€™000 for his protection. It is also possible to buy and sell CDSs without the underlying being actually part of the investorâ€™s portfolio. In line with the previous terminology, this is called a â€œnakedâ€ CDS. In essence, such naked CDSs allow traders to speculate on the creditworthiness of the issuer of the underlying. Naked CDSs actually constitute most of the CDS market. As will be shown in later chapters, they also played a significant role in the most recent sovereign debt crisis. As a consequence, since 1 December 2011, the European Parliament has banned naked CDSs on the debt for sovereign nations.
ï€¦ Key concepts Derivative markets, forward contracts, futures contracts, commodity futures, interest and exchange rate futures, swaps, options, call option, put option, strike price, option writer, option holder, covered option, naked option, in-the-money, out-of-the-money, credit default swaps. ïƒ¾ Questions for review â€¢	 â€¢	 â€¢	 â€¢	 What are derivatives and which types do you know? Market efficiency

15	 Market efficiency
15.1	 Learning objectives
In this chapter, we first try to understand the different forms of efficiency. We then proceed by examining the nature of behavioural finance.

15.2	

Forms of efficiency

After having reviewed the theoretical evidence for a variety of markets, the question arises: is it possible to â€œbeat the marketâ€ systematically? And, if so, how easy is it to do so? The so-called â€œefficient market hypothesisâ€ (EMH) â€“ a theory that is widely known in the financial literature â€“ claims that, first, financial prices are always in equilibrium and, second, that it is virtually impossible for any investor to consistently beat the market. To see this in more detail, suppose, that, first, all investors have free access to all currently available information and, second, all investors are well acquainted with the existing models of financial analysis. Following these assumptions, it seems reasonable to expect that all investors immediately adjust their holdings if new information arises and, hence, an asset price will always be reasonably close to its â€œfair valueâ€ since it can be expected to adjust almost immediately to any new development. In other words: in such an (information-)efficient market, any relevant information is immediately reflected in asset prices.202 It is obvious, however, that in order to arrive at such a conclusion the information set available to the investor is of crucial relevance. Accordingly, the financial literature has discussed three forms, or levels, of market efficiency. A market can be regarded as being â€œweak-form efficientâ€, if actual asset prices promptly react to and fully reflect all available information of the past history. The relevant information set, therefore, comprises the complete history of asset prices and, accordingly, it should not be possible to earn excess returns in a systematic way by trading on the basis of past price developments.203 A market can be regarded as â€œsemi-strong efficientâ€ if actual security prices promptly react to and fully reflect all publicly available information.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Market efficiency

ïƒ¾ Testing for market efficiency â€“ the Runs-test The question whether stock prices really follow a random walk can be addressed by means of the so-called â€œRuns-testâ€.204 Assume a â€œ+â€denotes an increase in (daily) stock prices, whereas a â€œ-â€ stands for a similar decrease. Suppose further, the following time series could be observed: (++++) (------) (+++++++) (------) There are four increases, followed by six declines, seven increases and another six declines. Taken together, there are four runs.205 In the next step, the question arises, whether these four runs and 23 observations differ from the ones that could be expected on the basis of a strictly random sequence of 23 observations. The test for the randomness of runs can be done as follows: we define n as the number of overall observations (= n1 + n2). Moreover, n1 stands for the number of â€œ+â€-symbols and n2 for the number of â€œâ€“â€œ-symbols, respectively. Finally, k represents the number of runs. Then, under the null hypothesis that successive outcomes are independent and assuming that n1 > 10 and n2 > 10, the number of runs is (asymptotically) normally distributed with (i)

The relevant information set, therefore, comprises the past history of prices and, in addition, all publicly available information such as, for instance, the companyâ€™s annual reports, its ad-hoc press statements, but also more general news regarding the current state and future evolution of the macroeconomy. It should, therefore, not be possible to earn excess returns in a systematic way by trading on the basis of publicly available information. A market can be regarded as â€œstrong-form efficientâ€, if actual security prices promptly react to and fully reflect all publicly available and private information. The relevant information set, therefore, comprises both, public and private information and, hence, it should not be possible to earn excess returns in a systematic way by trading on the basis of inside information. Tests for the efficiency of markets usually show that asset markets are highly efficient (but not strong-form efficient) and capable of processing relevant information into asset prices at a very high speed. Therefore, investors cannot easily expect to earn abnormal profits by trading on publicly available information.206

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Market efficiency

15.3	

Behavioural finance

The view of highly efficient asset markets is, however, increasingly challenged by the popular field of behavioural finance, which has nowadays evolved into a separate branch of financial analysis. More specifically, behavioural finance highlights certain inefficiencies and among these inefficiencies are under-reactions or over-reactions to information as causes of market trends and â€“ in extreme cases â€“ of bubbles and crashes. Over time, also psychological effects have been incorporated into behavioural finance. In line with this, such mis-reactions are then attributed to various causes, among them limited investor attention, over-confidence or over-optimism, herding instinct and noise trading. Taken together, numerous examples of unexplained abnormal returns have been identified. Indeed, various studies have shown that stock prices move too much to be explained solely on the basis of changes in fundamentals.207 Therefore, the debate about the validity of the â€œcorrectâ€ degree of market efficiency will continue.
ï€¦ Key concepts Concept of an (information-) efficient market, forms of market efficiency, weak-form efficiency, semi-strong form of efficiency, strong-form efficiency, implications of market efficiency, behavioural finance. ïƒ¾ Questions for review â€¢	 â€¢	 â€¢	 â€¢	 What is the exact meaning of an efficient market? Which forms of market efficiency do you know? Is it possible to beat the market on a permanent basis? What is at the core of behavioural finance?

Banks and other financial institutions

16	 Banks and other financial institutions
16.1	 Learning objectives
In this chapter, we first try to understand the nature of commercial banks and other financial institutions and the respective business models. We then proceed by examining the related challenges and opportunities.

16.2	

Commercial banks

Besides the central bank, there are a number of other financial institutions participating in financial markets. In stark contrast to central banks, however, these are profit-seeking institutions in the sense that they follow a specific business model. Perhaps the most important participants are commercial banks. From an economic point of view, a commercial bank is a financial institution whose primary activity consists in taking deposits from a variety of customers and granting loans to a variety of borrowers.208 A typical bankâ€™s balance sheet shows on the liabilitiesâ€™ side the sources of funds and on the asset side the use of funds. In a schematic illustration, it would look as follows
Assets Securities Loans Other assets Liabilities Deposits Other liabilities Shareholdersâ€™ equity

Stylised balance sheet of a commercial bank

From a general perspective, the two largest positions on the asset side are securities and loans. Marketable securities are relatively liquid assets that banks can trade at any time in financial markets. They usually include government bonds, corporate bonds and other kinds of bonds. Loans typically include loans to non-financial corporations, consumer loans and real estate loans. Other assets usually include physical assets, such as, for instance, buildings. The main share of the liabilities consists of deposits from customers. The difference between banksâ€™ assets and banksâ€™ liabilities is generally called â€œshareholdersâ€™ equityâ€ or, equivalently, â€œbanksâ€™ net worthâ€. The latter expression is due to the fact that, by definition, shareholdersâ€™ equity is the remainder if all assets were to be sold and the returns were to be used to pay off the existing liabilities.

130
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Banks and other financial institutions

As already mentioned, from an economic perspective, the activities of a commercial bank mainly consist in taking short-term deposits from customers and to use them for granting loans and buying securities. Against this background, it is not surprising that, in order to earn a profit, the interest rate paid on deposits has to be lower than the ones charged on loans. From a time perspective, however, banks often borrow at short maturity from depositors and lend at longer maturity to households, firms and governments. As a result, banks face a so-called â€œmaturity mismatchâ€, since the maturity of their liabilities is much shorter than the maturity of their assets. Banks are, therefore, subject to a liquidity risk arising from the fact that â€“ unless they are able to raise funds elsewhere â€“ they might have to sell relatively illiquid loans at the current market price, when they are confronted with sudden large deposit withdrawals (i.e. so-called â€œfire salesâ€). More generally, there is widespread agreement in the economic literature about the fact that banks face a number of risks. More precisely, four kinds of risk can be identified, namely a â€œcapital riskâ€, a â€œliquidity riskâ€, a â€œcredit riskâ€ and an â€œinterest rate riskâ€.209 As just mentioned, the liquidity risk refers to the risk that a bank may not be able to properly deal with unusually high cash withdrawals. The credit risk refers to the fact that borrowers might default on their loans. Given the fact, that borrowers have â€“ under normal circumstances â€“ an information advantage about their projects and the related risks, banks might not be in a position to properly discriminate between â€œgoodâ€ and â€œbadâ€ borrowers. As we shall see in later chapters in more detail, this risk is often related to information asymmetries and related phenomena called â€œadverse selectionâ€ and â€œmoral hazardâ€. The â€œinterest rate riskâ€ refers to the effect that changes in interest rates have on the bankâ€™s balance sheet. The capital risk is closely linked to the bankâ€™s leverage ratio that is to the basic question of how much debt the bank is willing to accept in doing its business. In the literature, it is quite common to measure the degree of bank leverage by the ratio of assets to capital. It is then quite intuitive that a high leverage ratio must be seen as both a blessing and a curse: a high leverage ratio can magnify a small return on assets into a high return on a relatively limited amount of capital, but unfortunately exactly the same mechanism would hold for losses. In an extreme case, it is possible that a comparatively small loss could completely wipe out banksâ€™ own capital.

Banks and other financial institutions

16.3	

Other financial intermediaries

Since the 1990s, non-bank financial institutions, such as for instance investment banks, hedge funds and money market mutual funds have become increasingly important in modern financial systems and have in the public been labelled the â€œshadow banking systemâ€.210 While the latter system is quite heterogeneous in nature, the common characteristics of shadow banks are that they are non-bank financial institutions that engage in â€œmaturity transformationâ€ by raising short-term funds and using them for buying assets with longer-term maturities. By contrast to commercial banks, however, they are not subject to a traditional banking regulation and, therefore, cannot borrow from the central banks in case of emergency. At the same time, the shadow banking system seems in essence to be more vulnerable to shocks than commercial banks since they operate in a less regulated area. While estimating the size of the shadow banking system constitutes a particular challenge since many of those entities do not report to government regulators, it is clear that the size of the shadow banking system by far exceeds the size of the commercial banking system. Notwithstanding the fact that investment banks share the label â€œbanksâ€ with commercial banks, in practice, their activites differ considerably. The services offered by investment banks include the underwriting of debt and equity offerings and the intermediation role between the issuer of securities and the investors. In addition, they act as market-makers, are involved in mergers and acquisition activities and corporate reorganizations and act as brokers for institutional clients.211

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Banks and other financial institutions

As already mentioned in earlier chapters, a generally accepted definition of a â€œhedge fundâ€ does not seem to exist. This notwithstanding, there are some common characteristics, which might be of help in describing the activities of hedge funds. To begin with, one could in a very simplistic way define a hedge fund as a potentially highly-leveraged private investment vehicle that could follow a wide range of investment strategies with the aim of achieving high absolute rates of return in all market situations. Against this background, a hedge fund is usually run by a number of partners, who typically try to raise capital from wealthy individuals and institutional investors. It is expected that the hedge fund partners also inject some of their own capital into the fund in order to create an adequate incentive structure. While the regular annual fee is often quite low in many cases, partners often take away a substantial part of the profits made by the fund.212 Hedge funds can follow a variety of strategies, among them â€œmacro strategiesâ€ that place major bets on developments in specific economies, and â€œequity market neutral strategiesâ€ that specialize in long-short portfolios of equities by buying undervalued assets and selling overvalued assets. Other strategies refer to bond prices, exchange rates or commodity prices or they focus on specific country groups, such as, for instance, emerging markets or specific financial market segments, such as, for example, convertible arbitrage funds.213 Event-driven strategies try to take advantage of one-time events by taking positions in, for instance, corporate bankruptcies and reorganizations but also mergers and acquisitions.214 From a general perspective, hedge funds are known to make heavy use of derivative instruments to build up highly-leveraged positions that would benefit substantially if markets were to follow certain directions and, thereby, would help to earn considerable profits.
ïƒ¾ The rise and fall of LTCM The â€œLong-Term Capital Managementâ€ (â€œLTCMâ€), located in Greenwich (Connecticut), was a fund that was founded by John W. Meriwether and had two nobel prize winners, Robert C. Merton and Myron S. Scholes, in the board of directors. Underpinned by complex mathematical models, the trading strategy of this fund consisted in taking advantage of a narrowing of spreads between various types of bonds by means of arbitrage activities. According to some sources, LTCM operated on a rather small own capital basis (around $2.3 bn) and, using a high leverage ratio, fared quite successfully over several years. In 1998, however, a default by the Russian government led to a â€œflight to qualityâ€ in international capital markets and, as a consequence, corporate bonds fell in price, while prices of government bonds rose. As a matter of fact, spreads widened substantially and LTCM started to incur huge losses in less than four months. In the end, the fund had to be rescued by a consortium of 14 financial institutions, among them the investment banks Merrill Lynch, Goldman Sachs and J.P. Morgan.
Source: Lowenstein (2000).

Banks and other financial institutions

16.4	

Leveraging and deleveraging

As already mentioned, the phenomenon of leveraging has the potential to amplify profits and losses. In fact, leveraging makes perfect economic sense if the costs of borrowing are smaller than the income that can be expected from leveraging the asset. By contrast, deleveraging can be defined as a process that aims at reducing the leverage ratio. It goes without saying that such a process can occur both at the microeconomic and at the macroeconomic level. The latter case, however, bears more severe consequences for the economy. This is due to the fact that in case various sectors of an economy decide to simultaneously set a process of deleveraging into force, this can have significant macro-economic effects and the potential to lead into a serious recession.215

If it really matters, make it happen â€“ with a career at Siemens.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Banks and other financial institutions

In order to deleverage, debt needs to be eliminated from the balance sheet, which in principle can be done either by raising capital or by selling assets or by carrying out both activities at the same time. Such a process of deleveraging usually starts shortly after the start of a financial crisis and often lasts for several years. In this context, typical patterns seem to exist as firms and households generally deleverage their balance sheets by increasing net savings and cutting expenses, whereas financial intermediaries often shed assets and cut down lending, which inevitably leads to tighter borrowing constraints and, possibly even to a credit crunch. In parallel, in difficult times quite often an additional â€œflight-to-quality behaviourâ€ by lenders and investors emerges as they start to seek for less risky investments. Such a behaviour can then even trigger further adverse effects if firms that are in financial distress are forced to sell assets rather quickly and at any price, which in turn causes asset prices to further collapse. Finally, it is possible that the collapse in asset prices spreads further into consumer prices, thus setting deflationary tendencies in motion. Taken together, it is certainly not exaggerated to say, that â€“ besides other forms of cycles â€“ economies also seem to follow typical â€œleveraging-deleveraging-cyclesâ€. As a consequence, leverage tends to become too high in boom times and too low in bust times, thereby oscillating around the fundamental value of assets. Against this background, such cycles must be seen as important amplifying mechanisms of credit and business cycles that contribute to drive down the values of asset prices substantially in market downturns.

16.5	

Banks runs and banking crises

While in many parts of the world, no major banking crises have been observed for a long period, this should not be a reason for complacency. In essence, the banking system remains a fragile construct. It is true that under normal circumstances, there is no serious problem for banks arising from the funding side. The bank is normally able to have at least a broad estimate of how many deposits are withdrawn each month. Under exceptional circumstances, however, if a large number of customers were to decide to withdraw their deposits from a financial institution, the latter might be confronted with the serious danger of insolvency. In fact, such dynamics have the potential to turn into a â€œself-fulfilling prophecyâ€, since the more people try to withdraw their deposits, the quicker the bank runs out of cash reserves and, thus, the probability of default increases.
ïƒ¾ Fat tails in financial data As we have already seen in earlier chapters, some financial markets data are characterised by a certain degree of skewness and kurtosis compared to the normal distribution. In the literature, such a behaviour is also often labelled as a â€œfat-tailedâ€ distribution.216 This observation is equivalent to saying that compared to a normal distribution, rare events can happen more often. In fact, while a daily change in the Dow Jones of seven percent should, assuming a normal distribution, only occur once every 300â€™000 years, it is known that in the course of the 20th century, it happened 48 times. At the same time, a number of asset price models, among them the well-known Black-Scholes option price model rely on the assumption of an underlying normal distribution. If the actual distribution is a fat-tailed one, then the model will, for instance, underprice options that are far out of the money. In a nutshell, it can be concluded, that market participants are vulnerable to hazardous â€œBlack swan eventsâ€ and exposed to losses beyond those that are predicted by their existing financial models.
Source: Hudson and Mandelbrot (2004), Taleb (2007).

Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Banks and other financial institutions

Moreover, the danger of â€œcontagionâ€ emerges, if bad news about one bank spread further and affect the reputation of other banks. In fact, once a bank has experienced a bank run, depositors of other banks have a strong incentive to withdraw their money from their banks as fast as possible to avoid losing it when other banks are also forced to close down. In such a case, other banks will be forced to sell loans and securities at any price, and the combined sales efforts of many banks will drive asset prices even further down. Moreover, if several banks suffer such bank runs at the same time, a so-called â€œbanking panicâ€ might emerge. It is important to note in this context that â€“ at a certain stage â€“ facts are not of relevance anymore. In fact, bad news or rumours are fully sufficient to initiate a bank run and bring the bank into trouble. This is equivalent to saying that the stability of the banking system rests in essence on the confidence of its depositors. If the latter believe that a bank is in trouble, then the bank will eventually come into trouble.

16.6	

Key features of the banking system in the euro area

When gauging the economic importance of the banking system, the size of a banking sector can in a first approximation be described in terms of the amount of assets that are held by banks and which reflect the activity of financial intermediation by banks, i.e. the provision of funds to the various sectors of the economy.

Banks and other financial institutions

Against this background, the aggregated balance sheet of the credit institutions in the euro area was â‚¬14.233 bn at end-1998 and â‚¬30.747 bn in the beginning of 2014.217 However, the assets and liabilities of the banking sector vis-Ã -vis the rest of the economy are considerably less than the above-mentioned figure, since many assets and liabilities relate to activities between credit institutions. At the same time, total banking assets amounted to 176% of GDP in 1985 when measured in terms of the total assets of credit institutions resident in the countries which now comprise the euro area. At end1997 this figure already stood at 234% and in end-2013 total assets were more than 300% of euroâ€“zone GDP. This indicates that the growth of the banking sector has been significantly faster than that of the economy as a whole. Furthermore, there has been a general increase in derivative, guarantee and other off-balance sheet activities of banks that do not enter into the above measurement; hence the changes in total assets do not fully reflect the changes in the volume of services offered by banks. At the same time, the aggregated total assets of the euro area MFIs are still dominated by loans. At end-1998 the overall share of loans to euro area residents in total assets was 73% and 54.2% in the beginning of 2014. From a general perspective, over the past decades, competition in the banking sector has increased considerably, both in in the euro area, but also at a global level. The latter development can be attributed to a number of fundamental forces pressing for change in the financial system. Among those factors, financial liberalisation, innovation, technological progress and the diversification of savings and investment portfolios have to be mentioned. It goes without saying that the introduction of the euro has clearly acted as a catalyst for further competition and structural change in the banking sector by speeding up the emergence of a genuine single market in financial services. From an institutional perspective, the euro area banking sector remains very fragmented and dominated by national and sometimes even local characteristics. While, in some countries, a large part of the banking activity is concentrated among a few dominant players, in other countries, the market share of banks that operate on a nation-wide basis is rather small. The total number of credit institutions in the euro area was 8.249 at the beginning of April 1999, 7.059 in the beginning of 2103 and 6.790 in the beginning of 2014. This high number basically reflects the large number of savings and co-operative banks â€“ often operating at the local level â€“ and specialised credit institutions in a number of countries.218 By contrast, the trend decline in the number of credit institutions reflects the ongoing consolidation process within national banking industries and, more recently, also the effects of the global financial crisis. Consolidation has been a global phenomenon in the financial services industry and has been driven by a more intense global competition and the need to increase efficiency and reduce costs. The consolidation trend is also common to almost all euro area countries and has significantly increased the concentration of banking activity in the largest institutions at the national level, especially in smaller countries.

Money supply and money demand

17	 Money supply and money demand
17.1	 Learning objectives
In this chapter, we try to understand the key features of monetary aggregates and to explore the determinants of money supply and demand. We also aim at becoming familiar with the channels of monetary policy transmission and the related time lags. We then consider the monetary policy instruments in the euro area. Finally, we investigate monetary policy in times of financial crisis and reflect upon the way forward.

17.2	

Monetary aggregates

So far, we have been talking about money, but what does money exactly mean in practical terms? This is indeed not an easy question and the answer is by no means straightforward. The latter is due to the fact that in todayâ€™s world, a variety of different financial assets exist that perform the functions outlined in earlier chapters. Moreover, many of these assets are close substitutes and the fact that their nature tends to change over time, makes it difficult to come up with a clear definition of how exactly money should be defined. Following international conventions, however, most central banks define and monitor at least a narrow, an intermediate and a broad monetary aggregate. In this context, the different types of money are usually classified as â€œMâ€s. More specifically, M1 consists of the currency in circulation and private non-banks overnight deposits. M2 represents money and some â€œclose substitutesâ€ for money, such as, for instance, certain forms of time deposits. M3 adds in addition some longer-term deposits, such as, for instance, savings deposits to M2. Another important monetary aggregate is the so-called â€œmonetary baseâ€ (or, alternatively the â€œcentral bank money stockâ€ or â€œhigh-powered moneyâ€). It consists of currency in circulation (CU) and the reserves held by commercial banks with the central bank (RE). This yields MB = CU + RE. Why is the monetary base so important? This is due to the fact that the central bank can almost directly control this monetary aggregate. In order to show this, let us have a look at a simplified and highly stylised balance sheet of a central bank. The latter looks as shown below:
