18.5	

The Taylor rule

In 1993, the US-economist John Taylor proposed a simple monetary policy rule that rapidly gained a lot of popularity. In its simplest form, the rule can be expressed as follows:302 (18.5.1)	

i t = rt *+ ÊŒ t *+ Ä® Â˜ (ÊŒ t - ÊŒ*) + È• Â˜ (y t - y*)

where ÊŒ t stands for the inflation rate, ÊŒ* represents the desired rate of inflation and (y t - y*) denotes the output gap. Moreover, Ä® , È• mirror the weights assigned to the objectives by the monetary authority. The rule, therefore, recommends a tight monetary policy when inflation is above its target and output is above its full employment level and vice versa. In case, both inflation and output are at their desired levels, the interest rate should just equal the equilibrium nominal interest rate, which is composed of the equilibrium real rate and equilibrium inflation.

.

186
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Click on the ad to read more

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Monetary policy in practice

In his original study, Taylor assumed a real equilibrium rate of 2% and a growth rate for potential output of around 2.2%. As regards the inflation objective, he selected a value of 2%. Finally, the weights were â€“ as a rule of thumb â€“ chosen to be of equal magnitude (i.e. 0.5). When applying his rule for the Fed over a period from 1987 to 1992, the rule seemed to mirror the Fedâ€™s behaviour so well that Taylor concluded: â€œIn this sense, the FED policy has been conducted as if the FED had been following a policy rule much like the one called for by recent research on policy rulesâ€.303 In related work, Taylor also showed that his rule is compatible with the quantity theory and, therefore, also with a number of monetary policy regimes followed in the past. Moreover, he emphasised that his rule showed a remarkably robust performance in a number of simulation studies aiming at the stabilisation of inflation and output. Taken together, he suggested to use his rule as a device to identify â€œpolicy mistakesâ€.304 The Taylor rule has a number of advantages. First, it is simple, easy to understand and intuitively convincing. Moreover, the determinants of central banking behaviour are fully in line with the mandate of monetary policy â€“ at least as far as the Fed is concerned. Third, empirical estimates for a variety of countries tend to give the correct signs and plausible magnitudes. Insofar, the Taylor rule seems to show a rather good empirical â€œfitâ€. Finally, the rule can â€“ under certain assumptions â€“ shown to be micro-founded.305 This notwithstanding, a number of disadvantages have to be mentioned. First, a number of uncertainties remain, among them the selection and concrete choice of important variables, such as, for instance, the real equilibrium interest rate or potential output. Second, while a number of authors interpret this rule as a â€œreaction functionâ€ of a central bank, it is de facto a â€œreduced formâ€.306 Third, the information set of a central bank also includes monetary and credit data, exchange data and many other variables, and is, therefore, much richer than the information set used in a Taylor rule. Fourth, some studies have shown that Taylor rules can be a source of instability for economic systems. In case, forward-looking expectations are used, multiple equilibria could result.307 In case, backward-looking expectations are used, economic systems tend to be more stable but they are less convincing from a theoretical point of view. Fifth, the Taylor neglects some key considerations of practical monetary policy. It is, for instance, a wellknown fact in monetary policy-making that a central bank should react to different kinds of shocks in different ways. In reality, it might often pay to abstain from a policy reaction for the moment and to adopt a policy of â€œsteady handâ€, if, for example, a pass-through of the central banksâ€™ interest rate reaction along the yield curve should be avoided. The latter behaviour is, however, not captured in the simple framework of a basic Taylor rule. Finally and perhaps most importantly, it has been shown that most studies are based on ex post data, i.e. data that have often been revised after a certain time. However, central banks tend to base their decisions on real-time data, i.e. data that are available at the time of the decision-making process. The use of (possibly revised) ex post data in Taylor rule estimations might, therefore, yield misleading results.308

187
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Monetary policy in practice

Notwithstanding these considerations, Taylor rules can be seen as very useful indicators and guideposts. They have, however, to be taken with some caution and should not be applied in a strictly mechanical way. More recently, the Taylor rule framework has been extended in various ways.309 A first important extension consisted in the introduction of the so-called â€œTaylor principleâ€. 310 The latter postulates that the weight assigned to the inflation term must be larger than one, since a less-than-proportionate reaction of the central bank to an increase in inflation might lead to a decline in real interest rates, thus fuelling inflationary pressures even further. This is why a central bank is well-advised to initiate a more-thanproportionate reaction in such a case.311 Further studies have tried to change the basic framework by both introducing additional variables and changing the underlying specification. For instance, a number of studies have tried to include an exchange rate variable as an additional determinant into the rule. This choice can be motivated either by the existence of some sort of exchange rate constraint or by the attempt to better mirror the economic situation faced by some countries, for instance, by small open economies. Other authors have made an attempt to better integrate a forward-looking Taylor rule framework with the interest rate-setting process initiated by the central bank. 18.6	

Monetary indicators

Besides the theoretical findings mentioned earlier, a number of studies confirm that excessive money growth ultimately leads to inflation.318 It is, therefore, for good reason that many central banks tend to look at a variety of different variables, among them â€“ most prominently â€“ money and credit variables. Traditionally, the latter variables are expressed and assessed in terms of annual growth rates. This is (among other reasons) due to the fact that annual growth rates tend to attract most attention in the public debate and, therefore, provide a natural starting point for a central bankâ€™s communication. As emphasised already several times, a substantial money growth might signal imminent dangers for price stability. But when is growth too high? This is indeed a very good question and the answer depends on a variety of factors. Very clearly, for a proper assessment, a benchmark is needed. One way to derive such a benchmark is provided by the quantity theory discussed in earlier chapters. Expressed in terms of growth rates, the quantity theory can be summarised as follows: (18.6.1)	

Ç» M = Ç» Yr + Ç» P - Ç» V

Monetary policy in practice

In case, some â€œnormalâ€ or â€œtargetâ€ values can be specified for the variables shown on the right-hand side of the equation, it should be possible to derive a â€œnormalâ€ growth rate for money, i.e. a growth rate that would be compatible with no imminent risks for price stability. By contrast, a growth rate that exceeds this â€œnormalâ€ level significantly and over longer horizons should signal the need to take action. Indeed, the prominent role assigned to money in the ECBâ€™s monetary policy strategy is also signalled in the derivation and announcement of a so-called â€œreference valueâ€. The latter has been defined in terms of the growth rate of the broad (euro area) monetary aggregate M3. The choice of this aggregate is based on a number of empirical studies that strongly support the view, that this aggregate possesses a number of requested properties, namely, first, the existence of a stable money demand function319 and, second, satisfying leading indicator properties for future price developments for the respective aggregate in the euro area.320
ïƒ¾ The ECBâ€™s reference value As already mentioned, the ECBâ€™s reference value for the growth of M3 has been derived so as to be consistent with the achievement of price stability.321 The concrete derivation of the reference value is based on the well-known fundamental relationship linking changes in monetary growth, inflation, real GDP growth and velocity. (i)	

âˆ† M = âˆ†Y r + âˆ† P - âˆ† V

When trying to quantify the proper values for the right-hand side variables, the ECB makes use of its definition of price stability as an increase in the HICP for the euro area of below 2% per annum, the medium-term assumptions regarding potential output growth (2%â€“2Â½% per annum) and the trend in the velocity of circulation of M3 (a decline of M3 velocity in the range of Â½% â€“ 1% per annum). Taken together, the results pointed to a decline of M3 velocity in the range of Â½%â€“1% per annum. On the basis of these assumptions, the ECBâ€™s reference value was set at 4Â½% per annum by the Governing Council in December 1998 and has not changed since then.322 The Governing Council decided to monitor the validity of the conditions and assumptions underlying the reference value, and communicates any changes to the underlying assumptions as soon as they become necessary.
Source: ECB (1999b), p. 40 ff.

It is worth noting, however, that a monthly growth rate that exactly corresponds to the reference value (being a growth concept by nature) is by no means a guarantee for price stability. This is due to the fact that the aforementioned considerations tend to neglect past developments. For instance, it is very well possible that money growth is on track at the current juncture, but has overshot over the past years. More generally, a central bank is well advised to also closely monitor the level of the money stock and various measures of excess liquidity. These measures constitute additional valuable information that might help to assess the overall picture of the liquidity situation. Various measures of excess liquidity exist in parallel. They all share the fact that â€“ by construction â€“ they are calculated by subtracting from the observed money stock various measures of equilibrium money balances and, therefore, abstract by their very nature from the short-term or dynamic aspects of the money demand equation and, thus, are by construction more medium term-oriented concepts.323

191
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Monetary policy in practice

The so-called â€œmoney gapâ€ is calculated as the deviation of the actual stock of M3 from the level implied by the reference value. By contrast, the so-called â€œmonetary overhangâ€ (or â€œshortfallâ€) is defined as the difference between the actual money stock and the equilibrium money holdings as identified by a suitable money demand model.324 But why do central banks sometimes react and sometimes abstain from taking action? This is due to the fact that monetary developments might have their origin in different kinds of sources, which have to be identified first in a proper way before triggering implications. On the one hand, monetary developments may result from current changes in the determinants of money (e.g. high current monetary growth caused by high current output growth or lower interest rates). For instance, a higher monetary growth resulting from real GDP growth above its sustainable level or an inappropriately low level of interest rates may signal inflationary pressures associated with an overheating of the economy. Developments in monetary aggregates may, therefore, be particularly useful to summarise valuable information contained in a variety of other indicators. If this is the case, using money as a convenient â€œsummary measureâ€ is likely to be of help for policy-making.325 On the other hand, it is easy to imagine that â€“ on some occasions â€“ uncertainties in, for instance, stock markets materialise. In line with previous considerations and, thereby, especially the fact that money must be seen as one asset in a well-specified portfolio, investors might for instance decide to temporarily withdraw from the stock market and to â€œparkâ€ liquidity in short-term assets, thus increasing the demand for money. The latter developments are, however, not related to the emergence of risks to price stability. In order to ensure that no misleading conclusions are drawn in face of such portfolio changes, it might therefore even be helpful to adjust M3 for such â€œportfolio shiftsâ€. In any way, the assessment of the information in money should take account of such identifiable portfolio shifts. But how can such a systematic assessment be carried out? One way to illustrate the systematic interrelationships between some of the key economic variables at the aggregate level involves the use of the so-called â€œconsolidated balance sheet of the MFI sectorâ€.326 The latter looks as follows:327
Assets I.	 Credit to euro area residents II.	 Net external assets (positions with non-euro residents) Liabilities III.	 IV.	 V.	 VI.	 Longer-term financial liabili-ties Deposits of the public sector Other factors Monetary aggregate (e.g. M3)

A simplified MFI consolidated balance sheet

Given the balance sheet identity, each item of the consolidated balance sheet can be expressed as the sum of all other items. From an accounting perspective, it then follows for M3: (18.6.2)	

I + II - III - IV - V = VI

For the monetary aggregates, this results in the following accounting identity: (18.6.3)	 		 		 		 		 		 Monetary aggregate (M3) =	 credit to euro area residents (I) + net external assets (II) â€“ longer-term financial liabilities (III) â€“ deposits of the public sector (IV) â€“ other items (V)

The net external asset position â€“ which captures the capital flows of the money-holding sector, where these are routed via MFIs, as well as the transfer of assets issued by the money-holding sector â€“ has at times exerted a non-negligible influence on money growth over recnt years. This notwithstanding, there are mainly three key monetary variables that deserve particular attention in monetary analysis for the euro area, namely M3, M1 and credit to euro area residents (or, more precisely, its sub-component called â€œloans to the private sectorâ€).

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Monetary policy in practice

M1 is a narrow monetary aggregate and it traditionally has the highest interest rate (semi-) elasticity of all monetary aggregates, which is equivalent to saying that the central bank can influence it more easily. Moreover, this aggregate most closely serves transaction purposes. At the same time, (real) M1 has reliable forecasting properties for (real) GDP growth for a period of four to six quarters ahead.328 The second aggregate is credit to euro area residents. It is probably fair to say that credit represents the most important counterpart to M3 developments. The subcomponent that can be deemed of most relevance are â€œloans to the private sectorâ€ and, thereby, especially the two elements â€œloans to householdsâ€ and â€œloans to non-financial corportaionsâ€. A simple correlation analysis suggests that (real) loans to non-financial corporations tend to lag behind annual real GDP growth by three to four quarters, while the growth of loans to households broadly coincides with annual growth in real GDP.329 In light of this lagging relationship, weak developments in loans can often be explained in a plausible way by a foreseeable downturn in economic activity, which in turn means that the weakness of loan growth can be explained predominantly on the basis of demand conditions. More precisely, the expected downturn leads to a decline in investment and, therefore, a decline in demand for loans. At the same time, this historical relationship between credit and activity basically reflects the experience with a broadly functioning financial sector and integrated euro financial markets. In the context of the financial crisis and related fragmentation developments, however, more and more credit supply considerations got into the focus of attention. It goes without saying that monetary analysis had to investigate the origin and nature of shocks to credit and loans more closely. The last monetary aggregate worth particular attention is the broad monetary aggregate M3. Given its nature, it is subject to both shifts within M3 and shifts inside and outside of M3. As already mentioned in earlier chapters, from a historical perspective, M3 is the aggregate that has the closest link to consumer prices. The previous deliberations have clearly shown that money growth that is driven by money supply factors is more dangerous for the ultimate objective of price stability.330 But how can we know whether it is money supply or money demand being in the driverâ€™s seat? At the end of the day, only the actual money figures can be observed, which cannot be identified as reflecting supply or demand. But why is it not possible to simply estimate money demand and if the observed figure matches the one predicted the money demand function, it can reasonably be assumed that it is all demand-driven and, therefore, not a problem? In essence, this is not a bad idea. In practice, however, it can be shown that the money demand models represent so-called â€œreduced-formsâ€, which means that they also contain elements of money supply and not only element of money demand.331

194
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Monetary policy in practice

How could a systematic approach to monetary analysis look like? The process starts with a first step, consisting of a detailed analysis of the most recent monetary data in terms of its components, counterparts and sectoral contributions. In this context, it is necessary to keep both, return and risk considerations in mind.332 This step is complemented by an additional step, in which the latest developments are compared with the fundamental situation, the latter being mirrored, for instance, not only in terms of money demand or loan demand models, but also in terms of current and expected developments in the real economy. In case, substantial deviations occur, views about the underlying reasons and, in paticular, their persistence have to be developed in a third step. Finally, conclusions vis-Ã -vis the outlook for future price developments have to be drawn.
Chart: The Monetary Analysis Process

Analyse latest monetary developments

Compare with fundamental developments

Explain source and nature of deviations

Derive implications for future price developments

Source: Gerdesmeier (2001), p. 13.

As regards the comparison with the fundamental situation, a variety of modern tools have been used in this respect, among them money demand models, money-based inflation forecasts, principal component analysis, dynamic factor models and many more. It goes without saying that they all have strengths and weaknesses. On the other hand, there are a number of obstacles which render the task clearly more difficult: external shocks, the ongoing structural changes, liberalisation and globalisation may partly or fully be reflected in the instabilities in velocity. As a result, there are no simple, automatic mechanisms but monetary analysis needs to be continuously evolving since it needs to tackle new phenomena with suitable tools on an ongoing basis. Consider, for instance, the particularly illustrative example of securitization. Securitization is a process that emerged in financial markets in the 1970s, but became more popular in the late 1990s and early 2000s. Securitization activity has at times reached breath-taking dimensions in the United States and, albeit to a lesser extent, also in Europe.

195
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Monetary policy in practice

ïƒ¾ Securitisation The expression â€œsecuritizationâ€ stands for a popular process in the financial sphere that consists of the pooling of (initially illiquid) financial assets or groups of assets (such as, for instance, residential mortgage loans) and their subsequent sale to a special-purpose vehicle (â€œSPVâ€), which then â€“ through financial engineering â€“ issues fixed income securities (so-called â€œasset-backed securitiesâ€) for sale to investors. In such an environment, the principal and interest of these securities, which are normally paid to the investors regularly, depend on the cash flows produced by the pool of underlying financial assets.333

The individual securities involved in such securitization activity are often split into several â€œtranchesâ€, whereby each tranche has a different level of risk exposure. More generically, a senior and one or more subordinated junior tranches can be distinguished. It is worth noting that the senior classes have first claim on the cash flows the SPV receives and the more junior classes start to receive payments only after the more senior classes have been fully repaid. In essence, this process can be thought of as a kind of â€œcascading effectâ€ between the classes of securities involved, which has in the literature often been referred to as a â€œcash flow waterfallâ€. In light of their lower risk exposure, the senior securites are typically AAA-rated, while the subordinated tranches, facing a higher risk exposure, usually receive a lower credit rating.

Monetary policy in practice

At first glance, securitization activity has a number of advantages. For instance, issuers are now in a position to transfer risks away to other entities being more willing to them on board. At the same time, investors are now able to invest in a pool of high-quality assets â€“ given the nature of the various tranches â€“ to achieve a better portfolio diversification. From a central bank perspective however, it is important that â€“ by its very nature â€“ the process of securitization involves the removal of substantial amounts of assets from credit institutionsâ€™ balance sheets to third-party financial companies, which might pose specific problems to the regular analysis of montary and financial data carried out within central banks.334 This led to the fact that â€“ in euro area data â€“ MFI loan data had to be adjusted for loan sales and securitization activity in order to get a better picture of the underlying dynamics. Moreover, the fact that loans can be removed from the banksâ€™ balance sheets suggests that securitization could make the bank lending channel less effective.335 This is â€“ among other things â€“ due to the fact that the response of banks to a monetary tightening by restricting lending supply will not be as severe as in the past for big, liquid and well-capitalised banks. Instead they could maintain the credit relationship with the client by simply bundling together some loans into tradable securities and selling them on the secondary market. Taken together, monetary analysis always has to be carried out with a solid dosis of macro-economic and monetary knowledge as well with great caution. It must be based on a detailed analysis of components, counterparts and sectors. This is due to the fact that different sectors show different behaviours and are subject to ongoing transitory amd structural changes and shocks. Cross-country variations might further complicate the situation and the assessment of different variables and, therefore, have to be taken into account appropriately.

Asset price bubbles and monetary policy

19	 Asset price bubbles and monetary policy
19.1	 Learning objectives
In this chapter, we try to understand the typical sequence of an asset price bubble and explore some historical examples in more detail. We then proceed by illustrating some measurement issues related to asset price booms and busts. Finally, we investigate various types of early warning indicator models and reflect upon possible monetary policy measures.

19.2	

Asset price imbalances

The dictionary defines asset prices as â€œthe prices of assets, including land and buildings, productive equipment and securitiesâ€.336 In recent decades, asset markets and their prices have increasingly caught the attention of academia and policy circles.337 The latter is probably due to a variety of reasons, among them the fact that the last decades have seen prolonged build-ups as well as sharp declines in asset prices in many markets (such as, for instance, equity and housing) and in both the industrialised and the developing world. In many cases, also serious adverse and long-lasting consequences for the macro-economies of these countries could be observed. This has led to heated debates regarding the appropriate monetary and regulatory response to these dramatic shifts. Despite a widespread agreement about the often devastating effects of a bursting of an asset price bubble, it is not really clear what a â€œbubbleâ€ or a â€œfinancial market crisisâ€ in essence are. While the general notion prevailing in the public and also in parts of the literature is that an asset price bubble is somewhat â€œexcessiveâ€, the definition of asset price bubbles have in the literature also been discussed on the basis of more theoretical grounds.338 In line with these considerations, it seems useful to distinguish, at least conceptually, between those instances in which asset prices are driven by changes in current and expected future â€œfundamentalsâ€ (i.e. the present value of the discounted flow of returns) and those cases, in which asset prices deviate from those fundamentals, the latter case being often denoted as an asset price bubble.

198
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Asset price bubbles and monetary policy

More generally, a precise definition of an asset price bubble must embody three elements, namely (i) an element of deviation from fundamentals, (ii) an element of momentum and (iii) a psychological element. In fact, the literature has identified five stages of such an asset price bubble.339 â€¢	 Displacement: the process enters into its first stage, when a sufficiently large outside event or shock materialises in at least one important sector, leading to a substantial change in expectations and anticipated profit opportunities. â€¢	 Boom: in the second stage, asset prices start to rise, first at a relatively modest pace, but then over time, gaining considerable momentum. More and more investors try to take advantage of the new opportunities and enter into the market, thus driving asset prices even further up. Typically, also money supply and credit increase in this phase. â€¢	 Euphoria: in the third stage, the boom mutates into euphoria. Asset price valuations depart increasingly from historical benchmarks and fundamental levels and reach extreme heights. Attempts to explain why the traditional valuation measures would not hold anymore appear in the public debate. Typically, some activities start to exaggerate in this phase, leading for instance to over-borrowing, over-investment and over-consumption. â€¢	 Profit taking: in the fourth stage, considerable uncertainties and a generally rather heated athmosphere emerge causing some (insider) investors to sell out, take profits and leave the market. â€¢	 Crash and panic: in the last stage, asset prices collapse, leading to a widespread rush of investors out of the market. Margin calls and the continuous meltdown of investorsâ€™ holdings force them to liquidate their best securities, often at any price. Driven by the excess supply of securities, prices decline futher sharply. But why are asset price imbalances so dangerous? Recently a number of studies have been analysing historical boom-bust cycles in asset prices in order to detect regularities with regard to the costliness of booms and to assess the potential for identifying dangerous booms at an early stage. More precisely, an IMF survey analysed periods of bust in housing and equity markets and reached the following conclusions:340 â€¢	 Housing price busts appear less frequently than equity price busts. Housing price peak-totrough periods on average last longer than equity price busts (four years versus two and a half years). â€¢	 Price declines during housing (equity) price busts are in the order of around 30% (45%) on average. Moreover, 40% (25%) of housing (equity) price booms are followed by busts. â€¢	 The output losses associated with asset price busts are substantial. The loss incurred during a typical housing (equity) price bust amounts to 8% (4%) of GDP. â€¢	 Bank-based financial systems incur larger losses than market-based financial systems during housing price busts, while the opposite is true for equity price busts. â€¢	 All major banking crises in industrial countries during the post-war period coincided with housing price busts
199
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Asset price bubbles and monetary policy

Other work focuses on aggregate asset price boom periods. By distinguishing between high-cost and low-cost booms, the following facts could be derived:341 â€¢	 High-cost booms typically last around a year longer than low cost booms (four versus three years), lead to a build-up of larger real and financial imbalances and are accompanied by stronger real estate price booms and higher inflation towards the end of the booms. â€¢	 During the early stages of booms, real money growth and real credit growth are larger for high-cost booms. â€¢	 Towards the end of high-cost booms, the stance of monetary policy is typically looser than during low-cost booms.342 Given the potentially destabilising and devastating nature of financial market crises, it is quite natural that the academic literature has since many years focused on the design of so-called â€œearly warning indicator modelsâ€.

19.3	

Some historical examples

In this section, a brief overview over some particularly illustrative bubble episodes that could be observed over past centuries is provided.343 The expression â€œtulipmaniaâ€ (or, alternatively, â€œtulipomaniaâ€) dates back to a specific period in the history of the Netherlands.344 From a historical perspective, tulips probably arrived in the so-called â€œUnited Provincesâ€ in the late 16th century from Ottoman Turkey and soon became a luxury good. In this context, it is important to note some peculiarities related to (past) tulip cultivation.345 At the time, tulips were often subject to a mosaic virus, known as the â€œTulip breaking potyvirusâ€, whose effect was to produce remarkable patterns in form of vivid colours, lines and flames on the petals on the flower. The valued mosaic pattern could, however, only be reproduced by cultivating the buds from the original bulb and, therefore, a virus-infected tulip bulb represented de facto a monopoly on the value of future tulips of the same type.346 The price of one special rare typus of tulip bulb, called â€œSemper Augustusâ€, was 1000 guilders in 1623, 1200 in 1624, 2000 in 1625, and 5500 guilders in 1637.347 Another bulb was sold in February 1637 for 6700 guilders, while the average annual income at the time was only 150 guilders. It goes without saying that tulips were also exchanged for land, valuable livestock, and houses.348 By 1636, tulips were traded on the stock exchanges of numerous Dutch towns and cities by all members of society. During the winter of 1636-37, an unregulated futures market in common bulbs developed, when some traders started to sell tulip bulbs that had only just been planted or those they intended to plant (in effect, representing a â€œtulip futures contractâ€). This phenomenon was widely known as â€œwindhandelâ€ and took place mostly in local taverns of small towns between ordinary people, further fuelled by the fact that there was no margin requirement necessary to get involved in these deals.349 While the local authorities had banned all short sale contracts where the seller would actually not possess the underlying object, in practice, authorities did not prosecute people participating in proscribed futures contracts, but simply refused legal enforcement of such contracts.350 In February 1637, the bubble started to burst.351 Various attempts were made by the local authorities to resolve the situation to the satisfaction of all parties, but they failed.352 After the crash, prices are said to have fallen to less than 10 percent of their peak values and by 1639, prices had fallen to 1/200 of the peak price. It is reported that thousands of Dutch citizens were financially ruined. It is worth mentioning that â€“ according to some sources â€“ lesser versions of the tulipmania also occurred in other parts of Europe.

Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Asset price bubbles and monetary policy

An equally fascinating period was the â€œSouth Sea bubbleâ€, which was a speculative bubble in the early 18th century.353 It involved the shares of an English international trading company firm called the â€œSouth Sea Companyâ€ which was founded in 1711. This firm was granted a monopoly in trade with the Spanish colonies in South America and the West Indies in return for taking over the English war debt after the war of the Spanish succession. The enormous (potential) profits to be made from such trade raised the companyâ€™s stock price up to enormous heights before it afterwards collapsed spectacularly. More precisely, during the first half of 1720, the companyâ€™s stock price rose by over 700%, then fell during the second half of 1720 to about 50% above what it had been at the start of the year. The investor outrage led the Parliament to open an investigation into the matter in December 1720. In the context of this investigtion, a proposal was made in Parliament to place bankers in sacks filled with snakes and thrown into the Thames River. The â€œMississippi bubbleâ€ refers to the rapid rise and fall in the share price of a French company founded by the Scottish economist John Law (1671â€“1729), who despite being a convicted murderer, was promoted to the position of a â€œController General and Superintendent General of Finance of Franceâ€ under the rule of King Louis XV.354 In 1719, Law acquired several individual companies and merged them into a conglomerate called â€œCompagnie des Indesâ€, which became, however, more popular under the name â€œMississippi Companyâ€. John Law took the position of Chief Director of this new company. Given the fact that this company was given control not only over trade between France and its Louisiana and Canadian colonies, but also over trade with China and the East Indies, it in effect held a monopoly over all trade between France and the rest of the world outside of Europe.355 Investors, who at the time knew little about the distant colonies of Mississippi, were increasingly attracted by the rumours about the wealth of these colonies. The companyâ€™s expansion was basically financed by the issuance of shares. Fuelled by an effective marketing strategy and resulting purchases by all classes of society (including the King himself, but increasingly also people from the working class), the share price rose from around 500 livres tournois in January 1719 to 10,000 livres tournois in December 1719. In January 1720, however, share prices started to decline when investors decided to sell shares in order to turn capital gains into gold coins. Since there was not enough gold in the French kingdom to satisfy such an attempt, the company tried to get investors to accept paper money (instead of gold coins) and by trying to peg the share price at attractive levels. After a series of devaluations in the course of 1720, however, the share price dropped to a level of 500 livres tournois in September 1721, approximately its value in May 1719.

Following a three decades period of â€œEconomic Miracleâ€, Japan experienced a rapid run-up in equity and real estate prices in the late 1980s, which led stock and real estate prices to reach extreme heights. From 1980 to the all-time high in 1989, the Japanese stock market rose by around 373% (in real terms) but then crashed in a spectacular fashion and fell by 50% in the next three years.356 Land prices followed a similar pattern. They almost tripled in the second half of the 1980s, and, at its peak in 1990, the market value of all land in Japan was four times the land value of the United States. By the end of 1993, Japanese land prices had dropped by almost 50%.357 It is generally believed that the collapse of the bubble threw the country into a severe recession and long period of economic stagnation, which became more widely known as the â€œLost Decadesâ€. The expression â€œdot-com bubbleâ€ refers to a speculative bubble covering the period from, broadlyspeaking, 1997 to 2001. The main characteristic of thus bubble was a rapid increase in stock market valuations in many countries predominantly in the new Internet sector and related fields, which was then followed by a sharp decline.358 Two main developments can be seen as key characteristics for this period. At the microeconomic level, the period was marked by the founding (and later on, spectacular failure) of a group of new Internet-based companies commonly referred to as â€œdot-comsâ€. At the macroeconomic level, the period was marked by the notion of a emerging â€œNew Economyâ€, i.e. a regime characterised by a permanently higher rate of productivity growth.

The â€œdotcom bubbleâ€ finally burst on 10 March 2000, when the technology-dominated NASDAQ Composite index peaked at 5,048.62, more than double its value just a year before. By contrast, the Dow Jones peaked on 14 January 2000 and the broader S&P 500 on March 24, 2000. By 2001, the bubbleâ€™s deflation was in full strength. The bursting of the dotcom bubble marked the beginning of a lengthy, albeit relatively mild recession.

19.4	

Debt-deflation and the Minsky moment

The so-called â€œdebt-deflation theoryâ€ is an explanation that was developed in the aftermath of the Wall Street crash of 1929 and the subsequent Great Depression.359 In essence, the theory attributes the sequence of economic cycles and, thereby, especially the occurrence of booms and busts to the so-called â€œcredit cycleâ€ in general and to the overall level of debt in particular. More concretely, Fisher (1867-1947) developed the view of a dynamic process, in which falling asset and commodity prices create pressure on nominal debtors, forcing them into distress sales of assets, which in turn lead to further price declines and financial difficulties.360 For a long time, Fisherâ€™s work got forgotten since economists generally held the view that debt-deflation should be interpreted as a pure redistribution from one group (debtors) to another group (creditors). In the absence of substantial differences in the marginal spending propensities among those groups, it was perceived that such redistributions could not have major macroeconomic effects.361 More recently, a number of authors have revived these ideas.362 Another explanation came from Hyman P. Minsky (1919â€“1996), a US-economist who aimed at understanding and explaining financial crises. Minskyâ€™s model of the credit system, labelled as â€œfinancial instability hypothesisâ€ built upon the work of many earlier economists, such as, for instance, the Austrian American economist Joseph A. Schumpeter (1883â€“1950). Since Minsky formulated his theories only verbally and did not build mathematical models, his work did not receive proper attention for a long time. This changed, however, in light of the most recent crisis.363 In Minskyâ€™s view, the key factor pushing an economy towards crisis is the financial system and, thereby, especially the accumulation of debt by the non-government sector. In line with this hypothesis, Minsky identifies three different types of borrowers (that contribute to the accumulation of debt), namely the so-called â€œhedge borrowersâ€, the so-called â€œspeculative borrowersâ€ and the so-called â€œPonzi borrowersâ€, the main difference between them being the way they pay for their debt.364 Hedge borrowers are able to fulfil their contractual payments (i.e. the repayment of interest and principal of the debt) by the cash flows resulting from their investments. By contrast, speculative borrowers can only deliver the interest payments and, therefore, have to roll over the principal liabilities regularly. The cash flows of Ponzi borrower, finally, are not sufficient to make the necessary payments. It is for this reason that only a permanent appreciation of the value of the underlying asset can keep the Ponzi borrower in the game.

204
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Asset price bubbles and monetary policy

According to Minsky, it is the relative shares of these borrowers that are of relevance. If hedge financing dominates, the economy might well be in equilibrium. If, by contrast, speculative and Ponzi finance dominate, the economy is more likely to be in a critical state. At the same time, it seems a characteristic feature of boom periods that investors seem to be lulled into complacency. As Minskyâ€™s financial instability hypothesis suggests, when optimism is high and ample funds are available, investors tend to migrate from the safe hedge end of the Minsky spectrum to the risky speculative and Ponzi end.365 And if the use of Ponzi borrowing is widespread enough and asset prices stop appreciating, then the system goes into a bust period and, following a domino effect, might even bring the speculative borrowers and the hedge borrowers into troubles or even default. This slow movement of the financial system from stability to fragility and afterwards crisis gave rise to the popular expression of the â€œMinsky momentâ€.366 Taken together, Minsky claimed that â€œthe financial system swings between robustness and fragility, and these swings are an integral part of the process that generates business cyclesâ€.367
ïƒ¾ Ponzi scheme Carlo Pietro Giovanni Guglielmo Tebaldo Ponzi (1882â€“1949), also known as Charles Ponzi, was born in Italy. In the 1920â€™s, he became well-known in the United States for establishing a money-making scheme. The scheme was based on the promise of high returns by buying discounted postal reply coupons in other countries and redeeming them in the United States at face value. While officially labelled as a kind of arbitrage activity, in reality Ponzi was repaying the early investors by using the investments of the later investors. This so-called â€œsnowball systemâ€ ran for more than a year before collapsing. Later on, it became well known as the â€œPonzi Schemeâ€.
Source: Oxford Dictionary of Finance and Banking (2008).

19.5	

Detecting asset price booms and busts

Designing an early warning system for asset price imbalances can, in essence, be divided into three basic steps.368 In the first step, the asset price misalignments need to be defined. In this respect, a variety of approaches have been used to define such misalignments, ranging from purely statistical methods to more model-based approaches. In the second step, suitable variables are identified that could serve as leading indicators and they are then integrated in an appropriate model set-up. In the third and final step, the predictive performance of the indicators over a specific sample period is assessed and conclusions are drawn. In order to examine the effectiveness of individual indicators, one could think of assessing the performance of each indicator in terms of the following matrix:369
Crisis (within specific horizon) Signal issued No signal Issued

In this matrix, A is the number of periods, in which the indicator issues a â€œgoodâ€ signal, B is the number of periods, in which the indicator issues a â€œbadâ€ signal, C is the number of periods, in which the indicator failed to issue a signal, when the crisis occurred and D is the number of periods, in which the indicator refrained from issuing a signal when in fact, there was no crisis. A perfect indicator would only produce observations that belong to A or D cells. Similarly, an indicator that issues a signal when no crisis follows (as denoted by the matrix field B) is said to commit a â€œfalse alarmâ€ (or a â€œtype I errorâ€). If, by contrast, no signal is issued but a crisis follows (i.e. matrix field C), one speaks of a â€œmissed crisisâ€ (or a â€œtype II error). Although a variety of approaches are used in parallel in the literature, the usefulness of an indicator is often typically assessed by comparing the share of false alarms with the share of correct signals given by the indicator. In this context, the so-called â€œnoise-to-signal ratioâ€ (â€œN-t-Sâ€) is often defined as the ratio of the share of bad signals to the share of goods signals that is: (19.5.1)	

N-t-S =

B/ (B + D) A /(A + C)

A perfect indicator would the obviously minimize the noise-to-signal ratio.

19.6	

Early warning indicator models

In the literature, a number of generic approaches have been used to predict asset price misalignments. A first strand followed in the literature is the so-called â€œsignallingâ€ method, in which a signal is issued as a warning of a boom (or bust) over a specific period, whenever the selected indicator breaches a threshold. This is, for example, the approach used by Kaminsky, Lizondo and Reinhart (1998), who applied this specific approach to currency crises and others.370 More precisely, in their original study, the authors monitor the evolution of a number of economic variables. When one of these variables deviates from its normal level beyond a certain threshold value, this is taken as a â€œwarning signalâ€ of a possible crisis within a specified period of time, whereas a crisis is defined as a situation, in which a sharp fall in the variable of interest exists. It is obvious that the choice of the threshold is crucial for the validity of this approach. As a rule, threshold levels are often selected in a way that a balance is found between the risks of having too many false signals and the risk of missing too many crises. The second strand followed in the literature is the so-called â€œdiscrete choiceâ€ method, which in essence uses probit/logit regression techniques to evaluate an indicatorâ€™s ability to predict either a boom or a bust by estimating the probability of such an episode occurring within a given time frame.371 A warning signal is issued when this probability exceeds a certain threshold.372 Studies that follow this particular approach are often carried out on the basis of panel data, which have the advantage of incorporating information across countries as well as along the time dimension.373

The third strand followed in the literature focuses on structural models. More particularly, recent papers have focused, for instance, on the method of quantile regressions, applying them to the stock as well as to the housing market in the euro area.374 Such a procedure offers a number of potential advantages.375 In this context, an underlying model (based on fundamentals) is developed and tested. Booms and busts are then selected as episodes when the selected asset price index deviates excessively (i.e. for instance exceeds the 80th percentile or undershoots the 20th percentile) from the levels which would be implied by these economic fundamentals. Other approaches focus on a Markow-switching model, which identifies and characterises periods of crisis and no-crisis periods endogenously.376 It is also worth noting in this context that the results of the aforementioned methods and variables fit actually quite well with practionersâ€™ view. 19.7	

Monetary policy, asset and consumer prices

A possible impact from monetary policy actions on asset prices and then into consumer prices is not a new phenomenon. In fact, basically all monetary policy transmission channels postulate an involvement of asset markets before monetary actions â€œend upâ€ in consumer prices. As a rule, consumer prices are among the stickiest prices in the economy and they tend to absorb shocks only gradually. By contrast, asset prices are among the most flexible prices and tend to absorb shocks rather quickly. According to the literature, asset price developments might have effects on the real side of the economy through a variety of channels, such as, for instance, via wealth effects on consumption, via Tobinâ€™s Q effects on investment, via balance sheet effects and via exchange rate changes on net trade.378 These various channels can be briefly summarised as follows. â€¢	 Wealth effects: Looking through the several channels of the transmission mechanism, a surge in property or equity prices which increases private sector wealth will lead to higher consumption demand and may, finally, feed through to higher consumer prices. The latter effect is usually known as â€œthe wealth effectâ€. Such an effect might become more visible if the share of asset property in private portfolios increases and/or the higher the number of individuals that benefit from the rise in property or equity prices as a share of total population. In this respect, the literature seems to agree that (perceived) permanent rather than (perceived) transitory effects seem to matter. The exact classification of permanent versus transitory changes remains, however, an open issue and clearly warrants further investigation.

â€¢	 Tobinâ€™s Q effects: Tobinâ€™s Q-theory of investment represents another channel which links investment demand and equity prices.379 As discussed in earlier sections, Tobinâ€™s Q is defined as the market value of capital relative to the replacement cost of capital. For example, if equity prices are relatively high, the cost of replacement of a given capital good is lower than its market (equity) valuation and, therefore, there may be a stronger incentive to increase investment. If the increase in equity prices is of substantial magnitude and/or the number of firms that are financed by equity is significant, an investment boom might occur. â€¢	 Balance sheet effects: more generally, an increase in asset prices tends to make asset holders wealthier. This effect, however, also works in downward direction, fortified by existing information asymmetries. In an environment in which firms need to borrow funds against collateral, banks might â€“ in light of a decline in collateral and existing information asymmetries â€“ ask additional risk premia or simply cut back the supply of loans. In such a perspective, credit must be seen as the decisive hinge between asset prices and consumption or investment. The aforementioned effects are summarised in the stylised chart below:

When assessing the available quantitative evidence for the euro area, earlier studies seem to show that the wealth channel is the most important of the various channels.380 There is little evidence so far indicating that Tobinâ€™s Q, balance sheet and confidence considerations play any major independent role in the transmission of asset price effects to economic activity.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Asset price bubbles and monetary policy

As regards the spillover effects from asset price developments into consumer price developments, it is worth noting that, via its impact on consumption and investment, spillover effects into inflationary or deflationary developments might be generated. In parallel, risks to the financial system might emerge. At the same time, however, it is obvious that asset price developments must not necessarily feed into consumer price developments. This speaks against any kind of naive analysis. In the extreme case, excess liquidity can entirely go abroad and finance some investment there or â€œget stuckâ€ in the process.

19.8	

Monetary policy responses

The repetition of boom-bust cycles and the potentially very high costs for macroeconomic stability associated with the typically abrupt reversal of asset price bubbles beg the question: should monetary policy give more weight to asset prices? In this context, a variety of approaches have been discussed in the literature. To begin with, the two extreme cases would consist of a â€œdo nothing approachâ€ and an approaching aiming at â€œasset price targetingâ€. The first approach would regard boom-bust cycles as a characteristic feature of market economies that should not be tackled by policy intervention. The second approach would, in some sense, represent the other extreme: in order to avoid the negative outcomes associated with boom-bust cycles for the real economy, central banks should attempt to target asset prices. These two extremes are of course unrealistic for various reasons. A third approach considered has, for a long time, been broadly shared in the central banking and academic community. In essence, this view postulates that it is better to wait for the bubble to burst on its own and then to aggressively inject enough liquidity to avoid a meltdown in the banking system and the economy. In line with these considerations, this approach is called the so-called â€œmop up afterâ€ approach.381 While this approach can be seen as being supportive of both price stability and financial stability, it can â€“ at the same time â€“ be suspected of providing incentives towards the creation of moral hazard and excessive risk-taking in future boom times. Moreover, it restricts the role of the central bank to an entirely passive status during the build-up of a bubble and to the status of the â€œsaviourâ€ after the bursting of the bubble.382

211
Download free eBooks at bookboon.com

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Asset price bubbles and monetary policy

An alternative approach widely discussed in academia and policy circles consists of the so-called â€œleaning against the windâ€ strategy. According to this approach, monetary policy should be conducted in a â€œsymmetricâ€ manner over the financial cycle. In other words, it should be accommodative at a time of falling asset prices, but restrictive during a financial market boom. For instance, the central bank should conduct a slightly tighter monetary policy than warranted by its price stability objective, when the build-up of a potentially detrimental asset price boom is identified. By doing so, the central bank would â€œbuy insuranceâ€ against the risk of a harmful asset boom-bust cycle with its potential costs in terms of macroeconomic and financial stability.383 In other words: in order to maintain price stability over the medium to long term, it would accept a possibly higher consumer price variability in the short to medium term.384 In order to arrive at an overall assessment, various arguments have to be taken into account and carefully weighed against each other. On the one hand, it is a well-known fact that bubbles are extremely difficult to identify in real time. Assessing in real time, whether or not asset prices are being driven by fundamentals proves to be a difficult task that is surrounded by significant uncertainty. Moreover, the question arises whether the necessary steps in policy rates (that would be needed to curb boom and bust cycles in asset prices at times of overly enthusiastic expectations) could be of such a dimension that it would entail adverse implications for macroeconomic stability in the short term.385 This would clearly speak against a â€œleaning against the wind strategyâ€. On the other hand, recent studies have shown that there are indicators based on money and credit developments that can provide guidance on the nature and the consequences of extraordinary asset price developments, and, thereby, help to define the need for policy action. Moreover, as the current ongoing financial crisis seems to illustrate in a very impressive way, the macroeconomic costs of financial instability (and the challenges it poses for the maintenance of price stability) seem to provide reasonable support in favour of the case for a flexible â€œleaning against the windâ€ strategy.386

23	Glossary
Arbitrage: the simultaneous purchase and sale of a financial asset in order to exploit price differences and to realise a (risk-free) profit. Bank of England: the central monetary authority of the United Kingdom, located in London. Bank of Japan: the central monetary authority of Japan, located in Tokyo. Basis point: one basis point is equivalent to 0.01 percentage point. Behavioural finance: an area of financial analysis that focuses on certain market inefficiencies. Beta: a statistical measure that expresses the volatility or sensitivity of an individual stockâ€™s price relative to the price movement of the overall market. Business cycle: an expression that basically describes the fluctuations of real economic activity around its trend. The term â€œbusiness fluctuationsâ€ is often used as a synonym in the literature.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Glossary

Carry trade: a popular strategy in international financial markets aiming at making profits in foreign exchange markets by exploiting existing interest rate differentials across countries. Consolidated balance sheet of the MFI sector: a specific form of adjustment of the aggregated MFI balance sheet, whereby the inter-MFI positions (i.e. the inter MFI loans and deposits) are netted out. Convergence criteria: the criteria that a member state of the European Union must fulfil in order to join the European Monetary Union and, therefore, the Eurozone. Correlation coefficient: a measure of the degree of association between two variables. Crawling peg: an exchange rate arrangement, in which a predetermined rate of depreciation of the home currency against the foreign currency is foreseen. Credit default swap (CDS): a financial swap arrangement, in which the seller of the CDS commits to the buyer for compensation in the event of a loan default. Seen from that perspective, a CDS can be seen as being similar to a credit insurance. Currency area: a geographic area, in which the same currency holds. Derivative: a financial instrument that derives its value from an underlying asset, such as, for instance, a stock or a bond. Dollarisation: an exchange rate arrangement, in which a sound foreign currency is used either in parallel to the home currency or all on its own. ECB: European Central Bank, the monetary authority of the euro area. Economic analysis: one of the two pillars of the ECBâ€™s monetary policy strategy. In essence, this pillar tries to identify the short to medium-term determinants of price developments, with a specific focus on real activity and financial conditions in the economy. Efficient market: a market in which any relevant information is immediately reflected in prices. EMI: European Monetary Institute. EMU: European Economic and Monetary Union.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Glossary

EONIA: Euro Overnight Index Average, the effective interest rate prevailing in the euro interbank overnight market. Equity risk premium: the compensation an investor charges for the risk of a stock market investment. ERM II: Exchange Rate Mechanism II. EURIBOR: Euro Inter Bank Offered Rate. Euro: the name of the currency of the euro area. European Stability Mechanism (ESM): an intergovernmental institution established under public international law. European Union (EU): an economic and political union currently consisting of 28 independent member states. Eurosystem: the central banking system that consists of the 19 national central banks (NCBs) in the euro area and the ECB. Exchange rate: the price of a currency expressed in terms of another currency. Exchange Rate Mechanism II (ERM II): an exchange rate arrangement between the euro and the currencies outside the euro area. Exchange rate targeting: a monetary policy strategy that, in essence, focuses on maintaining a given level or change of the exchange rate. Executive Board: one of the decision-making bodies of the European Central Bank. Federal Reserve Board (Board of Governors of the Federal Reserve System): A committee that consists of seven members and establishes monetary policy in the United States. Federal Reserve System (Fed): consists of twelve regional Federal Reserve Banks located in major cities throughout the United States and a seven-member Federal Reserve Board of Governors with headquarters in Washington (D.C.). Fiat money: money that is given value only by a government decree.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Glossary

Financial market integration: Various definitions of this concept exist in parallel. However, most economists would probably regard financial markets as fully integrated when the â€œlaw of one priceâ€ holds, meaning that assets generating identical cash flows achieve the same return, regardless of the domicile of the issuer and of the asset holder. Fisher effect: a concept originally advocated by the US-economist Irving Fisher that explains the exact relationship between the nominal interest rate, the real interest rate and expected inflation. FOMC: Federal Open Market Committee. Forward contract: a private agreement, where one party agrees to buy (or sell) one commodity at a specific price on a specific future date and the other party agrees to sell (or buy). Frequency decomposition: a statistical technique that (in one of its variants) in essence decomposes a time series into three components, namely into a low frequency component, a business cycle component and a higher frequency component. Friedman-rule or â€œk-percent ruleâ€: a monetary policy rule, according to which the growth rate of money set by a central bank should be derived as to be in line with the growth in potential output.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Glossary

Futures contract: a contract, that is â€“ in principle â€“ similar to a forward contract but with some key differences. General Council: one of the decision-making bodies of the European Central Bank. Goodhartâ€™s Law: an empirical regularity, brought about by the British economist Charles Goodhart, explaining the apparent instabilities in the money demand functions observed for a variety of countries in the sense that a previously stable money demand function might prove unstable, when a monetary authority attempts to use a stable relationship for monetary targeting. Governing Council: one of the decision-making bodies of the European Central Bank. Graham, Benjami: the â€œfatherâ€ of value investing, an investment style that explicitly focusses on the difference between the price and the value of a company. Histogram: a chart that contains on its horizontal axis the variable of interest, whose values are divided into suitable intervals and the number of observations in that class is indicated by the height of the corresponding rectangles. Hyperinflation: A situation in which the economy shows a rapid (and often even accelerating) increase in the general price level that eventually gets out of control and, consequently, results in a rapid loss of the value of its purchasing power. Inflation: a sustained increase in the general price level. Inflation targeting: a monetary policy strategy that, in essence, focuses on the developments of the inflation forecasts in relation to a pre-announced inflation target over a specific time horizon. Interest rate: the price charged to a borrower for the loan of a certain amount of money. Interest rate parity: an equilibrium condition claiming a short- and medium-run equilibrium relationship between exchange rates and interest rates to hold. Issingâ€™s Law: a challenge to the well-known Goodhartâ€™s Law, proposed by the former Bundesbankâ€™s and ECBâ€™s chief economist Otmar Issing, arguing that the estimated money demand functions also contain elements of the money supply process, which might have the effect of a stable money demand function being possibly â€œcontaminatedâ€ by an unstable money supply process. More broadly, Issing also claimed that a credible and reliable monetary policy process might strengthen the stability of the financial system.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Glossary

Kurtosis: a statistical tool to measure the â€œpeakednessâ€ or flatness of the distribution of a series. LIBOR: London Inter-Bank Offered Rate. McCallum rule: a monetary policy rule first advocated by the US-economist Bennett McCallum. Monetary analysis: one of the two pillars of the ECBâ€™s monetary policy strategy. In essence, this pillar tries to identify the possible risks to price stability over medium-term to longer-term horizons. Monetary policy indicator: an economic variable that helps the central bank to judge the direction and strength of its monetary policy stance. Monetary policy intermediate target: an economic variable that can be controlled by the central bank with a sufficient degree of precision, thus allowing the central bank to base its monetary policy actions towards the behaviour of this intermediate target. Monetary policy strategy: the medium- to long-term procedure adopted by a central bank in order to allow the achievement of its ultimate objective. Monetary targeting: a monetary policy strategy that, in essence, focuses on controlling inflation by use of pre-announced growth rates for monetary aggregates as intermediate targets. Money: a good that provides three main functions, namely the function as a medium of exchange, as a store of value, and as a unit of account. Money market: the market for short-term funds, usually with a maturity of up to one year. Neutrality of money: a concept claiming that changes in the money supply will in the long run lead to changes in nominal but not in real variables. Optimal currency area (OCA): a concept that was pioneered by the Canadian economist and nobelprize winner Robert Mundell. Option: a contract in which the option seller grants the option buyer the right (but not the obligation) to enter into transaction on an underlying asset at some predetermined price within a specified period of time. Phillips Curve: a key economic concept that â€“ in the interpretation of Samuelson and Solow â€“ describes the link between inflation and unemployment in an explicit way.

Fundamentals of Monetary Policy in the Euro Area

Glossary

P-Star approach: a popular economic concept that, in essence, links the so-called â€œprice gapâ€ to the â€œvelocity gapâ€ and the â€œoutput gapâ€. Purchasing power parity: one of the oldest and simplest models of exchange rate determination, which is based on the idea of the â€œlaw of one priceâ€. The purchasing power parity in essence claims that a longrun relationship between exchange rates and price levels exists. Quantity theory: a key concept in economics that basically describes the link between money and prices. Scatterplot: a graph that contains two series, in which the values of the second series are plotted against the values of the first series. Seasonality: a pattern of cyclical variation occurring in a repetitive and predictable fashion. Such a behavior is not uncommon for many economic time series. Securitization: a process in the financial sphere that consists of the pooling of (initially illiquid) financial assets or groups of assets (such as, for instance, residential mortgage loans) and their subsequent sale to a special-purpose vehicle, which then through financial engineering issues fixed income securities (so-called â€œasset-backed securitiesâ€) for sale to investors. Skewness: a measure of data distribution that shows whether large deviations from the mean are more likely towards one side than towards the other. Swap: an agreement between two parties to swap something â€“ generally obligations to make specific payment flows. Most swaps today involve either interest payments or currencies. Standard deviation: a statistical tool to measure the variability or dispersion of a given data set. Taylor rule: a monetary policy rule first advocated by the Stanford professor John B. Taylor. Time series chart: a chart showing the time dimension on the horizontal axis and the variable under review on the vertical axis. Unemployment: status, when people are without work and actively seeking for work. VAR models: Vector AutoRegressive models. Yield curve: a graphical illustration of interest rates for similar bonds embodying identical risk, liquidity and tax considerations but different maturities.
